RATING: 9.5
Gave a 9 after the first play. I want to give it 10 but while it's been a blast so far I'm not 100% convinced it's an evergreen.

I did wonder whether the deduction side would become a routine exercise after several plays and become less engaging, and not everyone will enjoy the possibility of your whole game tumbling down if you mess it up, but for me these two things counteract each other. I just find it ridiculously fun and exciting. And once people start publishing... for a thinky Euro this game has drawn the most trash talk out of our group outside of The Resistance. But of a different flavour. "That can't be right! Someone debunk this garbage!" "Oh, but it is right. I think it is YOU who should check your working..." (aside: this is mostly my fault because in the first game I thought it'd be a good idea to rush grants with dodgy research, not realising how the end-game scoring worked...) 

edit: 10+ plays in and still love it, I think it's earned its 10

RATING: 7
submitted on another forum:

Spillede Alchemists for 2. gang i fredags. Har kun spillet det med min kone, men jeg må indrømme at jeg heller ikke er super interesseret i at spille det med flere. Det er et eurospil, med en god omgang deduktion smidt ind i spillet. Og så er det nødvendigt med noget elektronik (vi bruger vores iphones, 1 til hver, det fungerer rigtigt fint).

Man bruger sine cubes/workers til at købe ingredienser, opgraderings/bonus kort, brygge potions, sælge potions og lave "afhandlinger". Deduktionen kommer ind når du har de forskellige ingredienser, og du så skal regne ud hvilke egenskaber de har. Det er rimelig kløgtigt, men det gør også at der skal tænkes utroligt meget. Vores 2 personers spil har taget omkring 2 timer, men vi tager det så også meget afslappet. Jeg kunne bare forestille mig at med de folk vi normalt spiller med, så vil et 4 personers spil nok tage det dobbelte, og det tror jeg ikke spillet kan bære. 

7/10

RATING: 9.5
Juego de fusión de tablero con app móvil. Original en ese aspecto, no sé cómo no han salido más juegos así al respecto (esperando el Épica de Fenolín junto a la tablet :) )

El juego es de deducción, probando combinaciones de 2 ingredientes para ir eliminando los resultados erróneos. Detective alquímico xD. Y también de adelantarte a los planes de los contrarios, o fastidiarles demostrando sus errores en sus publicaciones XD

Con la mezcla de 2 ingredientes, creas pociones, que las puedes vender a aventureros, dárselas a probar a tus becarios xD, o bebértelas tú mismo para ver cuales son sus efectos, y con todo ello ir publicando teorías sobre los ingredientes o refutar las teorías de otros, para ganar como alquimista reputación o hacérsela perder a otro xD. También puedes comprar objetos mágicos que darán ventajas y/o puntos de victoria que junto a la reputación, las teorías correctas y las becas que hubieras conseguido, determinarán el ganador. De 2 a 4 jugadores, funciona perfecto, si bien con más jugadores hay mas piques :)

RATING: 7
Would be higher, but there is something I really dislike about how selling potions works. Getting beat out and not being able to sell a potion when you want to feels bad to me and this seems to happen a lot in two player games.

The game is also quite the heavy game.  It is basically two entirely different games to teach.  The deduction portion of the game can take a lot to teach for people not used to deduction games, and then the worker placement part of the game is quite involved as well, and does not follow standard worker placement 'norms' for how they resolve and where the 'workers' go.  Oh, then the publishing and debunking which almost feels like a THIRD game to teach.

Once the rules are done and around turn 3 or 4 when everyone understands what is going on, the it is GREAT FUN!  Game 2 with new players is WONDERFUL, but getting through game 1 is a huge hurdle, especially as our gaming nights usually involve alcohol, heh.

RATING: 9.5
It is quite common for cooperatives titles to offer systemic difficulty for the players to grapple with. After all, game systems are the only divide a player normally has from success. However, it is the rare competitive game that taxes players with a similar sense of difficulty, and for this Alchemists stands almost unique in my collection (Trajan and Macao are two others which spring to mind).

The balance between mastering the systems, and besting your competitors is perfectly honed in Alchemists. The cheeky humor, tight action selection options, and persistent desire to do more than you can manage makes this a stellar example of 21st century game design. 
  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Provisional 8 after one play. There is a lot going on here, and while picking up a new game usually comes very easy to me, I know this one needs additional plays before I can really give a fair review. I won with like 39 points (39-38-36 final score in a 3 way game) and we all made a TON of mistakes, including forgetting to score the high number of seals a couple of end rounds, and lots of just suboptimal plays. That being said, I really can't wait to play it again and I'm glad it's on the 10x10.

later: multiple plays, still quite good. Early publishing is important but risky. I've lost games from an incorrect hedging. I need to play more often to see if a metagame evolves.

The artwork is great and helps you get into the theme.

RATING: 6
I really admire many things about this game: the depth of the deduction challenge, the excellent art, the elaborate cardboard components, and the evident dedication to implementing a very strong and creative game design concept which, in this case, includes interfacing with an App.  

However, playing the actual game was simply not that much fun, and for me on my first time game, felt more like a chore than entertaining.  Sure, I want to solve a puzzle tonight as much as the next person, but do I yearn to solve a singular puzzle sitting on my cellphone screen, or would I rather have fun playing board games tonight?

It is my sense that this game is going to fall flat with many gamers who play it for the first time.  Some, like myself, will have problems with key component readability in low light, trying to discern the difference between a plus and a minus sign within a small blue circle, etc.  Some will want to really solve everything on their turn, and run through all the permutations, and thus be gripped by times-a-wasting  AP, which will anger the other waiting players.  

Others will mistakenly place a "result circle" in the wrong place on their deduction grid and thereafter completely screw up their theories, feel like they wasted three hours due to a mere clerical error. And some first timers will simply give up trying to figure out how the combination cancellations and neutral results occur using colors, circle sizes, and plus and minus signs. Who really cares about abstract colors, circle sizes, and signs combining?!  I do?  Not really.

I do like the betting on other people's theories aspect of the game, the student experiment mechanic, and the discount bidding spot.  

I feel like I should want to play this obviously very interesting design again, but for better or worse, I actually do not want to play it again, so gonna have to give it a 6.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This game has two extremely unbalanced cards. The one where it gives you information once per round on an enemy's potion is just broken at 4 players, and extremely strong at 3. At two players it may be ok. The other gross one is the one that allows you to keep one ingredient instead of discarding both.

To give you an idea of how broken they are, in a 4 player game just having one of those meant having full information of every ingredient by the end of the game, while i had information on about 2-3 out of 8 ingredients. And every play I made was on getting information about ingredients for the whole game. Having that item means having at least 250% information compared to another player.

The explanation takes too long and the game could have been made simpler. Ingredients should have been free for instance. Otherwise, to fix the game as it is, just remove said cards or increase their cost to at least 5.

RATING: 8.4
A clever game of mixing potions, deducing the alchemical properties of the ingredients, selling said potions to adventurers, publishing your theories, and then scoring at conferences and a final round exhibition. The mechanisms for player order and placing action cubes are nifty, and the app works rather well for the potions once you wrap your head around the idea. Excellent, colorful artwork, good components (including elaborate player screens with their own deduction boards), and the rules have a little of that CGE sense of humor you'll recall from Dungeon Petz, etc. I do feel the game is already substantial enough without the Master Variant for debunking theories, but otherwise it's pretty damn good and the variation of ingredient properties with the app gives it hefty replay value.

RATING: 3
What a depressing experience. 

On the good side: the theme is very interesting and funny, the mechanics and fluff go welltogether. Also, at first sight, the illustrations are quite nice. The app works as intended, with a little fiddling even in low light.

But on the bad side, where do I start? It is a big mess. Feels as if there was the App and then they built a game around it and did not care about making it even slightly elegant.

It takes ages to explain, but there is not a lot of complexity, mostly it's just complicated. :(

Easily the most overhyped game of 2014.

RATING: 4.8
Initial rating, based on playing once. I'd rate the game experience by itself one point higher than my rating here; that point difference is for the app-requirement.

The game is gorgeously put together, with a lot of love and attention to detail. Sadly the game experience flows haltingly, slowing down to all the deduction and choices depending on the choices of the previous player, so you can't plan far ahead. Also, the app works pretty badly when the lighting isn't optimal, taking forever to recognize the cards (we switched to manual input after about a third of our first game), which makes things drag on ever more. Several actions in the game can be incredibly punishing when you've made a mistake, or just timed badly and are prevented by other players from taking the action you were hoping for. Also, if you're good at logic puzzles (as I am), you have an immense advantage over players who aren't. (I won 47-19-5 in my first game; that's not the type of score which makes for a fun game.)

The rulebook is "funny", but incredibly longwinded. Everything could've been explained much more succinctly, making it much easier to look up specific rules.

Reminds me in many ways of Dungeon Lords - and that isn't a positive recommendation for me.

RATING: 10
Very original and refreshing game! Very surprised with the quality of the components, excellent! One of the best deduction games out there!
The app is really necessary in this game. This seems to have a bit of the CGE's game mechanics in here, and also artwork. The artwork is from the same guy who did "Dungeon Petz". The select player order and draw cards accordingly is very similar to "Last Will". The sell to adventurers potions is very similar to sell petz to costumers from "Dungeon Petz". Vlaada did have a participation on the game after all.
Despite complaints have not found this game to be too long, nor does it drags itself. It plays at a very good pace, the typical round goes like this: usually all players go for at least 1 ingredient card (takes 20 secs for all players), then 1 player uses the sage (takes 2 secs), then 1 or 2 players sell, (takes 5 mins max - because of dedution aspects), then the artifact buy (takes 30 secs - reading included), debunk is not often used (not on the first 3 out of 6 rounds), then 1 or 2 players publish or endorse theories (takes 1 min), then players test on student (may take 10 mins if all players go there), and lastly the drink action, just 1 person usualy goes there (takes 1 minute). So I really don't see how the game drags. This game is underrated here on the geek.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Szerintem ez az egyik legfigyelemreméltóbb eurogém az utóbbi időben. Az már önmagában kuriózum, hogy egy telefonos alkalmazást hívunk segítségül. Féltem, hogy nem fér majd meg ilyen módon egymás mellett a technika és egy társas, de szerintem ez bőven belefér, érdekes színt ad az egésznek, egyáltalán nem zavaró. Másrészt a játék témája, mechanizmusa egyedülálló, már a szabály olvasásakor átütött az, hogy nagyon egyben van az egész. Letisztult. A végén azt írja a köszöneteknél a szerző, hogy Chvatil is ránézett, ez érződik is szerintem. Többünknek is beugrott róla a Dungeon páros. Talán a grafika hasonló, talán egy kicsit a sorrend sáv, a humoros körítőszöveg stb.
Az egzaktság, amit már írtam, azt hiszem el is várható egy ilyen témában. Egy olyan keverési mechanizmust talált ki a szerző, amiben néhány alaptételből következik minden, és az összes akciótípus ezekre a keverésekre épül. Ha két összetevőt összekeverünk, abból 7 dolog jöhet ki végeredményben: 3 jó, 3 rossz és egy semleges. Ezekből aztán rengeteg dolgot ki lehet következtetni. Végeredményben a cél minél hamarabb beazonosítani, hogy melyik összetevő melyik vegyületnek felel meg a 8-ból. Szerintem a tanonc fokozat nem lehet nagy kihívás, de a mester fokozatnál a cáfolás már nem mindig evidens. Matematikai logika órán jó szemléltető játék lenne szerintem. De nem feltétlen győz a logika, a játék sava-borsát az adja, hogy a lehető legjobb időzítéssel, esetleg némileg kockáztatva tegyük meg tétjeinket a megfelelő alkímiai elméletekre. Bízzunk a többiek tippjeiben? Vagy csak a saját következtetésünkben? Vezessük félre a többieket, bevédve magunkat (ez a bevédés a tévedés ellen zseniális szerintem)? Esetleg ők vezetnek minket félre? Nagyon sok finomság lehet a játékban, de az alapmechanizmusokra gyorsan rá lehet érezni.

Hogy rosszat is mondjak: A könyvelésre nagyon oda kell figyelni, az esetek kizárása azért nem csak annyi mint mondjuk a Cluedoban. A matekon kívül alaposság is szükséges szerintem, könnyű téveszteni, és sokat bukhatatunk rajta. Az újrajátszhatóságot nehéz felmérni, mester fokozatban szerintem benne lehet 10-20 parti, de több nem hiszem. Az eladás akció nekem túlbonyolítottnak tűnik, valahogy ezt feleslegesnek érzem, vagyis nem jöttem rá miért gyúrtak rá erre ennyire: a 4 smiley-s kártya, és a hírnév sávon a fokozatok... Kicsit túl van lihegve szerintem ez az akció.

Összességében egy nagyon jó és szép tematikus játék. Igazi jóféle, egyedi, de csak azoknak ajánlom, akik a dedukciós matekolást szeretik. Nem egy rövid játék, és nem könnyű.

RATING: 8
People can have different opinions about the guys at CGE, but nobody can state they don't think outside the box!

Alchemists is set in the same funny and clever world of Dungeon Petz and Dungeon Lords, a world that seem imagined by the genius of Terry Pratchett. The rulebook explains a quite heavy game with the usual humour and covers rules in such a way to let players have no doubts: there are many details, but everything makes (in a weird nonsensical way) sense. So, remembering the details is less difficult than on would believe.

Of course the selling point of the game is the interaction between the app and the physical game. I was skeptical, but I must admit I am very impressed: the app is just a "helper", assigning an "alchemical combination" to each ingredient, and providing a smoother and faster resolution to the combination of ingredients.

The game is challenging and interesting, and very engaging like almost any other CGE published game.

A final note on the production value: it is a gorgeous looking game, with lots of detailed artwork. The box is literally full of thick cardboard, and the player screens are very cleverly designed.

One of the highlights of 2014!

RATING: 4
:d10-4:
UPDATE: What a rating drop! 8 after the first play, 6 after the second, and now down to 4.

There isn't enough room to deduce information from the other players, and optimal deduction strategy is contingent on good luck. The board play has a mess of problems, from redundant actions to non-interactive worker placement (a contradiction in terms?), to swingy randomness.

If this could be played in an hour, it would still be a 6. But at its current play length (~ 2 hrs.) and its underlying problems, I would reject an offer to play.

OLD COMMENT: MUCH better than expected! I'm hesitant to recommend it long-term, as there are a few niggling issues:

1. The logical deduction was pretty interesting (it felt like those intersecting row logic puzzles you can buy at the grocery store magazine rack), and I can see player reading bringing even more into the deduction once you grasp all of the mechanics going on and you begin tracking ingredients taken. HOWEVER, if the deduction becomes rote once players figure out optimal strategies, all that will matter are the other game mechanisms. This would take away one of the most fun portions of the game and contribute to the second issue. 

2. The board play is very fiddly, and there are unnecessary mechanisms (artifacts; one of the two potion tests) that could (should?) have been excised from the final version of the game. On top of this, random chance can leave you in a poor early position due to lack of gold, leading to a runaway leader scenario.

That said, the game was interesting enough that I would be happy to play it a few more times with an equally experienced group to find out. I don't think I'll be buying it to discover this for myself.

RATING: 8
[i]Posted: 9 OCT 17, Rated: 8[/i]
{2P: 7, 3P: 9, 4P: 8}

[i]Alchemists[/i] is one of the first "app assisted" games I have played.  I love the simple way players can coordinate the initialization of the app and then start to use it to examine outcomes.  Even better, there is actually a compelling game here beyond the app-gimmick.

On the surface, this is a very straightforward game: collect ingredients, mix them together, and try to figure out their "natures."  Now add in some tougher elements such as selling potions, publishing theories, and the last round display of your alchemical prowess.  These are all opportunities to prove you know what you're doing.  That money from selling is useful because there are money considerations including buying artifacts!  And one-time abilities called "favors."  And the competitive nature of space selection is all gated by the opening bid for player order.

In short, there's a lot here beyond just scanning cards with your phone.

When describing the game, each of those mechanics sounds like it was just bolted on.  When playing the game, they all melt together and a seamless experience emerges.  It has taken me several plays before I finally feel like the deduction algorithms "clicked" in my brain, and even still I loved playing this game. 

The only things holding me back from a higher score are the intimidation factor for new players (there's a lot to this game), and how brutal the game can be with respect to action selection.  I also suspect that the game doesn't scale down as well.  I think it may be best with 3, still great with 4, and not as good with 2.  I also fear that the app may one day be abandoned, but it's still working today after nearly 3 years.  There's also an analog capability, but then you need a moderator.  Play this one while you can!

RATING: 8
One of my best purchases of 2015 and one of the most interesting mechanics I've ever encoutnered. The mobile app works great and I must honestly say that I was terribly wrong about it before I actually played the game. It's the best thing about Aclehmists and the sole reason the replayability of the game is endless. The second best thing abotu the game is it's theme. RATING: 6
A deduction game with a unique and clever smart phone interface that feeds back anonymous information on card selection. The question has been raised is this all gimmick and no game, well after one play I think the use of an iphone is essential because the idea of a player sitting out to neutrally decipher the information is unacceptable. I'm sure many games will follow this idea.

 Yes the game has a high luck factor, but you must be prepared to take a lot of chances otherwise a safe strategy will bring you in last.  Unless you chance new card combos, you won't get the results, unless you dabble with the bonus cards you won't get the extras that can make all the difference. Artifact cards that give a huge shot in the arm from game start or nothing at all except dubious point bonuses that don't compensate for the gulf between first and second place. My issues with the game are that this luck will reward you or punish you. There is no middle ground or way to odds things either way.

Too complex for a gateway game, too random for the gamer who prefers a heavy experience. Great production, great gimmick, massively random game that I wouldn't want to play again.

RATING: 6.5
Initial impressions:

A core of interesting kitsch with a lukewarm game built around it.

The central bit of deduction is the strongest part of the game; the fast pacing incentivizes players to put some guesswork in before committing, but the punishments for getting it wrong are quite severe, and can create a large amount of swingy-ness to the game by getting it right (or wrong) early.  Hedging bets seems like a waste of time (just one point), beyond getting the per-turn thumb bonus for the last few rounds.

Most of the worker placement/turn order sub-mechanics are interesting, but nothing new or particularly clever.

Taking random resources seems like a consistently bad idea with rare exception; getting specific resources to do specific tests is the most efficient way to gain as much information as possible with as few tests as possible.

Each of the artifacts is extremely powerful, with some being more powerful (conditionally) than others.  The villagers are completely dominant over the random resources (for action selection), and might not be properly adjusted for in the ranks.

In the one game I played, I was able to deduce every single element by turn 4, due to both my experiments and other players' results/bunking/debunking.  

I find the iconography of the symbols very poor, and rather too small.  I could not imagine playing this game very well with bad eyesight.  Something other than the large/small sizes should have been used, probably two unique shapes.

RATING: 7.5
Very cool, original game. I'm not crazy about the app dependence (and I have major worries that I won't be able to play this in 10 years), but the game itself is really interesting. The deduction mechanic is far, far more interesting than what you find in most games (e.g. Clue), and the art and theme really bring the game to life.

The rules for disproving theories (and "hedging against" a color) are also really confusing, and I still don't think I quite understand them.

---
Pretty sure the Magic Mortar is too strong. I've banned it in my games, at least until we're seasoned players. I would definitely recommend removing it for at least your first few plays. ---
I really enjoyed this - my favorite CGE game now, even probably ahead of Galaxy Trucker! Of course, you have to like deduction games obviously - it's not going to likely to change your opinion on that count.

Pure deduction on its own is not, in my opinion, enough to support a meatier game. This is why I like Sleuth for its brevity and economy, but am not enamoured of games like Black Vienna or Code 777 which try to be more interesting by simply making the deduction logic excruciatingly hard. The Alchemists OTOH takes an interesting deduction "engine" and then blends it with a real resource management game: you have to manage your cash flow, your supply of ingredients, you have to keep pace on publishing papers, and so on. If you suck at deduction you can't bail yourself out by managing your money wisely, but neither will your deduction skills save you from poor cash management.

The alchemical rules have taken some flak for not being intuitive, and it's true they aren't. But while not intuitive, they are not complicated either, and you just need to be really sure you understand them clearly before you get started. The combinations that create water are the trickiest, and the key piece of information (not super-clearly highlighted in the rules unfortunately) is that adjacent similarly-shaded rows on the sheet make water. Once you understand the engine here I don't think The Alchemists is actually as complicated as all that. But you do need to get it.

CGE games have a history of being hopelessly overwrought, but despite similar appearances, The Alchemists is actually pleasingly economical in terms of doing what it sets out to do. It's still complicated of course, but all the complexity is in there for a reason which can be easily understood and so the structure of the game is not hard to grok and - importantly - remember once a few core ideas are understood. The rulebook itself is a vast improvement over previous CGE entries, the jokes have been limited or sidebarred and it's more effectively focussed on communicating the actual design.

(I should say though that I haven't played the Master-level debunking rules, which seem to add a substantial chunk of complexity)

Anyway, I have a mixed history with both deduction games and with CGE, but this game I liked quite a bit. It is a CGE game, and it is deduction game, so obviously it's a niche. But it's also artful and well-executed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Usually I'm not one for games of this weight and length, but I love deduction games and the theme feels tailor-made for me, so I took the chance. I'm pleased to report that I find the game pretty damn enjoyable.

Figuring out how to most effectively deduce anything in this game allows for some really clever little tricks. It's less in the thought process for deductions and more in how to optimize your plays on the board for maximum deduction efficiency.

In general, the way you score points is all pretty fun and interesting stuff. The fact that points have an in-game use is nice, but moreover the strict turn / action limit means trying to score as much as you can as fast as you can makes for really nice pressure throughout.

The two player game and four player game are pretty different (I've not tried 3p yet). 

Four players feels more interactive, but also just feels more like a standard worker placement game. You can do less deduction individually and have to rely more on the greater WP strategies in order to succeed. I'm also kind of put off by the uneven number of player turns in 4p - this is not the kind of thing I usually gripe about, but in a game where turn order is [i]so[/i] important it feels wrong.

Two player feels a bit more relaxed as you have more time just to do your own thing and deduce things for yourself. As the deduction aspect is more pronounced with 2p, the game feels a little more unique here.

Controversial opinion time: But I think that because there is so much going on in the deduction portion of the game [i]and[/i] the severe turn / action limit you're up against that this isn't a game that really even benefits from increased player interaction. There's [i]so[/i] much going on in your mental space already that the mere thought of [i]someone else beating you to your latest deduction[/i] can send you into frothy rage. This is when the "race to see who can do something first" style of player interaction really works - the tension is ALWAYS high, even when formal interaction is low. Scoring points is precious, and seeing someone just just [i]one more[/i] causes some real anxiety in just the best way.

A complaint: The artifact cards just seem TOO powerful. A lot of the best cards are the first batch of cards available, putting you at a severe disadvantage for the whole game when you're NOT the one to acquire the best card. I know it's supposed to be incentive for competition, but the fact that in our two games so far the winner was someone who'd been helped along the entire game by a really great artifact just left a sour taste in my mouth.

Right now I'm really liking this game, but I'm not sure how much it will hold up over the long haul.

Also I can't get enough of that cat. Look at him! LOOK AT HIM. HE IS RIDICULOUS. When I am a mad, yet genius alchemist someday I will have a cat just like that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Cons:
- Takes a long time to teach.
- Some aspects of the game seem convoluted or poorly signaled. (fake publishing papers)

The main joy of Alchemists is in deduction. You receive new information as you progress and try to understand what is what. All the selling potions and publishing papers is just an extra strategic layer on top of this base joy. Sometimes that extra strategic layer doesn't feel compelling either. It's not immediately when it's worth it to publish a fake paper, for example. Were items really a necessary aspect of the game? Does there really need to be two different way to test potions (on yourself and students)? Some of these actions seem like complexity for the sake of complexity; it definitely doesn't feel elegant. On the other hand, without them, the game would just boil down to sudoku. So while both the deduction and strategic aspect of the game is enjoyable, neither are really that compelling.

In my opinion if logic puzzles are not fun for you then this won't be either.

RATING: N/A
I quite like: that the deduction isn't all of the game (just one driving factor); the publishing/debunking wars, (particularly the pressure to publish even when possibly (or definitely) wrong); the use of an app to avoid needing a moderator/GM for a hidden-information deduction game.

I'm lukewarm on: the deduction itself. It's clever in its nature and a fair bit of fun, but can also be pretty AP-inducing, and lucky hits early on can give some players much more information than others.

The rest of it... functions, and serves the game well enough, but doesn't feel especially coherent? Or maybe it does, but just doesn't grab me. A dash of blind-bidding for the potion sales, an artifact market for another way to use gold, Do A Special Thing cards... There's also several value cliffs (enough gold for an artifact, enough unused cubes for DAST cards) that felt more frustrating than interesting. The initiative mechanic is neat, at least. And there are some nice thematic touches throughout - expecting more from higher-Reputation alchemists, etc.

I have gaming friends who rate this a 10, and others who rate it a 3, and I can kind of see where they're both coming from. I'd play again if offered, but only if a turn timer or hard game-end deadline were involved.

RATING: 6
Components: Gorgeous artwork on the board and cards. Fun little potion bottle pawns. The app works fairly well.

Experience: First game at just over 4 hours, not counting rules explanation. That's way too long for what it delivers.

The core mechanism that attracted us, of trying to deduce the alchemical for each ingredient, is only a small part of this very complicated game that has bidding, resource management (the ingredient cards), worker placement, variable player powers (the artifacts), take that (some favors), and area control (publishing). Way too much going on for my tastes. If the game were a third as long, with half the complexity, I'd try it again.

Reminded me a lot of Dungeon Lords, in that the new, exciting mechanism (building a dungeon, deducing ingredient alchemicals) is overshadowed by a complicated worker placement game.

RATING: 6
Quite a polarizing game.  Our group played two games simultaneously.  About half said they would never play again, a few liked it and the rest were ambivalent.  Our game with 3 players, with two new players, took 3.5 hours with rules explanation.  The combination of deduction and worker placement tries to wed two almost-satisfactory elements into a greater whole, with mixed results.

I think the game would play equally well without the theme-infused elements like the artifacts and the negative effects of drinking your own potions. 

I would be curious to see a different game with the same deduction core.  Take out the fantasy theme and the workers, and just use the app and the ingredients.  You could make a 45 minute game out of just the deduction bits.

RATING: 7
So this is the sort of amazing Euro that mixed in a theme and *WOW* the theme really carries through. The humor comes out in the play and not just the presentation, which is a good plus. This speaks of truly inspired and skillful game design, IMO.

Setup time and putaweay time's are beastly... Just beastly, but this gets better, like any game, as you get to know it.

The components can be a little bit fussy (the cauldron especially), but the APP used in the game really is clean and works well.

Rule book has maybe two minor problems: YOU GET 2 GOLD TO START... but is otherwise well written AND entertaining, which is another rare combination. Truly good game. Rating may go up if it has legs.

RATING: 9.5
+ Awesome melding of deduction and worker placement
+ App keeps one player from being a woefully bored GM
+ Beautiful artwork with good piece production quality 
+ Moderately simple mechanics, yet deep enough to make each game challenging
+ Game duration/number of rounds is the perfect balance of being able to potentially discover all the components stats, but does not gaurantee it every game
+ Little to no turn downtime
+ High degree of replay-ability
+ There is a plateau at which a veteran player can reach as far as strategy, however, given the randomness of the resultants for every game, a newer, or even first time player can easily win against someone with multiple plays under their belt


- So many little pieces, with a lack of decent method to sort them with what comes in the box
- Sitting your cards on the cauldron screen is finicky, with cards falling through the slot more often than is acceptable
- Potion result tracking board does not hold the tokens quite snugly enough, causing any bumps to your cauldron screen to be catastrophic (which is bad if you're a klutz like me)
- The app camera CAN be finicky, but I've personally never had an issue with it. Keep your area well lit and it should never be an issue

RATING: N/A
[2015-02-14 First Impression]
I like [i]Alchemists[/i] although I seem to be very bad at deduction. I struggled through the whole first game just trying to deduce anything. It didn`t help that you also have to pay attention to the worker placement part of the game. The alchemists is one of these games that needs repeated plays in order to get the whole experience and this might proof to be a problem since there is quite a lot of rules overhead. This means it might not be that easy to get it back on the table which is a shame since I`d like to explore it a little more.
At least, I`ve played the deduction part a few times solo so that I won`t be totally clueless the next time I get the chance to play. And I do really want to give it another try!

:star: Hard to teach. Like in most CGE games, there are a lot of little rules. I guess they are necessary to keep the game interesting once you`ve figured out the deduction part. But this makes it hard to get new players interested in the game.
:star: The app is more of a tool than the central element of the game.
:star: The color choices of the playing pieces were a bit problematic for some players under bad lighting.
:star: The two hour playing time on the box seems to be correct after one or two games.

[2016-04-04 Second Impression]
It's been quite some time between my first and second play. As far as I can see, there are two reasons for this: 

First, this game is a monster to teach. The second time I needed nearly an hour to explain it to a seasoned gamer. It's not that it is very difficult, there are just a million little rules to explain. This is a huge hurdle when trying to get it to the table. At this point in my life, gaming time is very limited for me and my friends and sometimes we don't have more than two hours to play games a week. So, a long game with a long rules explanation just doesn't hit the table very often. This might not be a problem for you, but for me and my group it is.

The second point may be of more interest to anyone interested in my opinion of the game. I just feel that Alchemists could have been more streamlined. I'm a huge fun of games with little rules overhead and a lot of depth. In both my plays of Alchemists I felt like a hodgepodge of ideas was put into the game but somehow they do distract from the fun core of the game. Maybe most of the stuff might get valid and second nature in your tenth play, but currently it just keeps my friends from requesting the game again. A common complaint I've heard: "The game isn't rewarding enough for the time that is needed to play it".

Don't get me wrong: I'd still like to give a few more tries, but I fear that I won't get the chance to do so.

RATING: 6.85
This, I think, is about as close as a board game has come to approximating what real science is like.  You're in a race against the other players to probe, test, and experiment your way to understanding a part of the puzzle.  There isn't time to do the whole thing, and you need to get published to secure some of that sweet grant money, because funds are always tight.
It's difficult and thinky, and not something I'd want to do all the time.  The fact that you need an app, or to have one person sit out and be the moderator, is kind of annoying, but it wouldn't work otherwise.  If you're in a space with lots of games going on, you might be able to ask someone at the next table to take a few minutes out of their game when it's not their turn to help you.  And most everyone has a smartphone these days, so it's not such a huge problem.

Not really sold on some of the mechanics.  In our first game, I bought the coupon that discounts magical items, then nearly won by just collecting crap instead of playing the rest of the game.  But money is tight, and if the customers come out at the wrong time, you won't be able to sell them something they want.  
Also, the system for placing claims on the board is a little unintuitive.  You can either publish and score points, or hedge your bets and put out a placeholder.  The placeholder will get you grants, and let you publish, but isn't worth any points.  But you could also just take a 50/50 shot and put out your real one, gambling that your guess is right and risking losing points.  And if someone else puts out something you know is wrong, you have to burn your precious actions and resources disproving them and submitting your own claim.

I want to give this a few more tries and see how I feel about it then, but enjoyed it pretty well the early times.

RATING: 7
I enjoy this most because its good satire on academia. A funny and clever is possibly slightly over done game. At its core you are playing something similar to Sid Sackson's Sleuth except you need to indentify the three colour constituents for each ingredient by combining them in recipes. Over the top of this is a fairly full on worker placement game which meters out actions such as selling your potions getting extra ingredients and publishing your theories on the properties of a given ingredient for academic prestige. What makes it really great is you can sort of lie and get away with it in a game that isn't really about bluffing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
2015-05-12 : A simple deduction game with a huge luck element that takes twice as long to play as it should “thanks” to the iPhone gimmick. There are games that are really enhanced by having an app – games like One Night Ultimate Werewolf – and there are games that should have been designed without one – such as this game.

Basically this game boils down to : pick 2 random cards – check what the combination-result is – rinse & repeat. If by simple deduction you’ve narrowed the choices down somewhat, then is the time you need a specific combo of cards to get any further.  And those cards will or will not be available. If they’re not you can either do nothing or gamble. Gamble correctly and you win the game. As a deduction game this is worse than Clue.

And if the game didn’t have enough problems already, it’s pretty clear the sell potion / adventurer part of the board was already drawn before some last minute rule changes were implemented.  Not that those changes worked because adding another random factor - the discount cards - to an already random game really doesn’t help.

Most overhyped game of 2014 – by a mile.

RATING: 8
Everyone seems to have enjoyed this game. Everyone seems anxious to play it again. I was worried this game would end up being to complicated for a lot of people. That hasn't seemed to be the case. Or at least even the people who don't truly understand how to use their information (which seems to be the majority) still enjoy the game despite not fully comprehending it. It has a very Dungeon Lords feel to it, yet not as confusing. I love that the core of the game is a logic puzzle that you are trying to piece together and then leverage for points. 

Very neat design and good use of an app, that seems totally interracial to the game without being excessive. I will warn that I've seen the app mess up a few times, so make sure to double check your picture. And also make sure to double check your ingredients before taking the picture. Once you take it, you gain information, so you can't really take it back.

RATING: 7
In Alchemists, two to four budding alchemists compete to discover the secrets of their mystical art. Points can be earned in various ways, but most points are earned by publishing theories – correct theories, that is — and therein lies the problem.

The game is played in six rounds. At the beginning of the round, players choose their play order. Those who choose to play later get more rewards. Players declare all their actions by placing cubes on the various action spaces, then each action space is evaluated in order. Players gain knowledge by mixing ingredients and testing the results using a smartphone app (iOS, Android, and also Windows) that randomizes the rules of alchemy for each new game. And if the alchemists are longing for something even more special, they can always buy magical artifacts to get an extra push. There are 9 of them (different for each game) and they are not only very powerful, but also very expensive. But money means nothing, when there's academic pride at stake! And the possession of these artifacts will definitely earn you some reputation too. Players can also earn money by selling potions of questionable quality to adventurers, but money is just a means to an end. The alchemists don't want riches, after all. They want respect, and respect usually comes from publishing theories.

During play, players' reputations will go up and down. After six rounds and a final exhibition, reputation will be converted into points. Points will also be scored for artifacts and grants. Then the secrets of alchemy are revealed and players score points or lose points based on whether their theories were correct. Whoever has the most points at the end of the game wins.

RATING: 9.625
I love, love, love:
- the application of testing and deduction and the scientific method (especially in Debunking)
- the nod to the current state of academia
- the wonderful humor of testing on students, buyers, and ourselves!
- the art, personal cauldrons, and deduction trees
- the thrill and trepidation of publishing theorems you're not always totally sure about
- spying on your archenemies with a Periscope, seeing them lose reputation as they frolic naked in the streets, and more
- the bidding war for the opportunity to sell to a willing sucker--I mean, well-informed buyer
- the WP mechanism of choosing all actions last to first then carrying them out
- the theme of alchemists, alchemicals, magical items, and academics
- the equal focus on a Euro game and a deduction puzzle
- the relative simplicity of the deduction puzzle
- that there's a Master variant to make the game harder
- the rulebook is funny and very well written

I dislike:
- that the deduction sheets are not very easy to see

Final verdict: Great mix of humor, theme, worker placement mechanisms, planning, and deduction.

RATING: 9

While without hesitation I'd say that the original version is the best, the other variants are still interesting to play.

RATING: 8.03
Alchemists is one of the most AP-inducing games I own, to the point where I am terrified of the prospect of playing it with the full player count.  It blows the AP of Five Tribes completely out of the water.

Still, the deduction game of figuring out the alchemicals is really engaging, especially when played with the advanced rules where some educated guessing becomes more necessary.  The game is also richly thematic in a way that brings me great joy.  As a former academic myself, I chuckle a lot when teaching the game - I love the little touches like testing potions on students, but the students want payment once something bad has happened, or the way you can knowingly publish nonsense and it's OK unless someone else questions it.

So, I rank the game very highly for the theme and the unique deduction puzzle.  However, when you start to master the deduction puzzle, other parts of the game start to show some cracks.  The worker placement can feel somewhat fragile, and the early game can be dominated by luck.  If I happen to make a potion in round one that I have enough ingredients to duplicate, and a hero wants that in the second round, I've got a huge leg up on all of the other players.  If I take a gamble on testing on a student, and someone else makes a bad potion first, I could be falling way behind in my knowledge of what's happening.  It can feel pretty arbitrary and random sometimes.  I also find the potion selling space to be the most frustrating in the game.  I really dislike the way bidding works, and how the space is limited so not everybody can sell potions.  It gives that one step of the game a mean streak that feels different from the tone of the rest of the game.

Still, despite the flaws, I find Alchemists really fun, and think the theme is delightful.  I don't play it frequently, but appreciate it as a really good game when I do find the time to dedicate to it.

RATING: 7
Wow this one is thinky, and brain burning. The alchemical matrix is straightforward but there are a lot of options as each component is one of 8 alchemicals and you are hoping to narrow it down by testing the ingredients in pairs.

I actually really enjoyed the deep brain burning level although even halfway through the game I had to keep asking exactly what my results meant i.e a positive potion means that the two ingredients both have a positive for that colour and one is big and one is small. It is actually a lot to keep track of and you have to be very careful because if you mess up what your test result means it can send you off down a completly wrong track.

The rest of the game consists of ways to gain money which in turn allow you to publish papers and gain reputation (victory points). money is scarce and victory points hard earnt. 

I really really enjoyed this one, (and a big thank you to Paul Grogan of CGE for teaching us how to play)

RATING: 7
I wasn't immediately interested in playing Alchemists when it was released because of the smartphone integration, but after coming to realize that the game is playable without the app (but avoids requiring a human player to DM the game) I knew it wasn't going to be an issue.  And, it wasn't! The app worked seamlessly with the game and Alchemists retained all the "board game" flavour that a box of cardboard and plastic should.  I know about myself that sometimes I really enjoy deduction games and other times they don't fly for me, so I was unsure whether Alchemists would work.  I really enjoyed reading the manual, however, as the humour did help digest a rather beefy game.  Upon play, I found I was rather overwhelmed by all the + and - symbols everywhere and began to go cross-eyed as I tried to figure out which ingredients reacted with other ingredients to create which colours and polarity symbols.  Plus, there were little fiddly things to remember about some of the WP actions, so that also frustrated my ability to keep everything in check.  By the middle I was getting it though, and the deduction element was intriguing.  It really is a game that has to be played once before you truly understand all the things going on and how they interconnect.  By the end, I felt the game definitely worked, but I was slightly more underwhelmed than I though I would be.  There was just enough in Alchemists that I found wonky that it fuzzied my enjoyment of the pure deduction parts of it - which also didn't build the way I thought they would.  There were several times when the deduction part really took a back seat to the bluffing, which was unexpected.  Anyway: Alchemists is a fine game but didn't create that sense of enjoyment in me that I think really should have been stirred given the theme.  I'd play again, though.  Not sure if my rating could go up, however.

RATING: 10
Edit: I'm increasing my score because I can't stop thinking about this game - the theme, the components, the gameplay, it's all so good! One of my favourite games!

It's a great puzzle to solve and the worker placement shell around it just adds to the strategy. The components are magnificent, the little shelf for your ingredients is a wonderful touch. It's on the heavy side and you really have to pay attention, but it's not "mathy;" if you take proper notes everything should be clear. The only thing holding this back is the length, however it doesn't feel long while playing. In fact, the turn limit is excellent and ends the game just when the puzzle is starting to get solved.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Rated as a 9 after my first play (2p) as this game is full of theme, appears to be balanced and has a nice mixture of deduction (potential for bluffing) and worker placement. 
The game plays well and the app is brilliantly intergrated with clear and logical interactions that make it easy to use. Its a shame about the print issues, but the gameplay clearly overshadows those (and replacement tokens are onroute to me) and after a few more plays i feel we will be able to put the rulebook to one side and really be able to enjoy this game to its full. 
As with most CGE productions the game comes with "expansion modules" in the box, so once you have got to grips with the apprentice game, the master version is just waiting to be explored -  this also allows for experienced and newbie players to share a game together; as these varients can be selected on a player by player basis if needed.
All in all, a great game that i hope will soon become a 10 for us. (December 2014)
* Rating Revision * - jan 16 ; score dropped to 8 as it just hasn't hit the table, be this due to game time, level of complexity meaning it's a task to teach to some newbies and the need to have an iPhone fully charged. We generally have struggled to get a game with our longer games and if we do get one to the table it's more likely to be caverna over this. Still a great, cleaver game but it's just a game we have struggled to play regularly.
Feb 17 - dropped another 0.5 as we haven't played in ages (like most of our games) and would need to start from scratch to learn how to play again really.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
After playing once, I lamented that bluffing was so obviously not worth it-- it took most of the tension out of the game. It turns out that I missed a rule (the most-published player is supposed to get points a few times during the game) and that the "Master Variant" makes debunking harder. Knowing CGE, I assume the "Master" rules are actually the game as designed, with the others meant as a tutorial.

Would play again, but even if the risk/reward ratio for bluffing were more tempting, it's hard to get excited about the bog-standard action selection mechanisms when half the game's appeal is innovation.

RATING: 8
Played the prototype back in April 2014.
The smartphone gimmick worked really well: set up a pair of ingrediens behind your screen and take a snapshot. Then the system will tell you the cross-reading of the match. Unfortunately you still have to mark the result on a board, but I see no reason why this bit couldn't go digital as well.

Obviously you still have to work out the relationship between the variables yourself, and this is where my brain refuse to play. So despite a simple and clever system, I didn't have much fun playing it.

RATING: 8
Yera

Juego que sigue la dinamica de "emplazar trabajadores" pero con una tematica original (desarrollar pociones), profundo, bastante elaborado y bien implementado. 

Es un gran juego. Calidades excelentes y una mecanica rica y elaborada... donde yo le veo cierto defecto es en el ajuste. 

En mi opinion, lo que ocurre es que la parte de la formulacion (la de los ingredientes y sus compuestos) es demasiado compleja. no me entiendan mal, no estoy quejandome por lo dificil, pero si opino que el juego se desarrolla a lo largo de muy pocos turnos como para que puedas conocer bastante bien la "piramide" de formulacion para esa partida (que ademas, y eso esta muy bien hecho, cambia de partida a partida), y a la vez la mayoria de acciones de emplazamiento del juego en cada turno (que a veces no lo parece, pero el juego no consiste solo en hervirte el cerebro con combinaciones de hierbajos y patas de conejo) son muy dependientes de que te enteres con la formulacion o no, y de que consigas los ingredientes que necesitas (para hacer pruebas o pocimas) que salen de un pool comun... 

en resumen, que te puedes pegar media tarde con la cabeza loca de que ingredientes necesitas para hacer la pocion tal o de los compuestos que forman la pata de rana... para que en el ultimo turno no puedas hacer la pocion para la exposicion porque el que va delante tuya se ha llevado el ingrediente de la pila que necesitabas. 

Asi que si me preguntan... "buen juego?" si. lo es. sin ninguna duda. "lo aconsejas para que me lo compre?" pruebalo primero. 

yo particularmente, para jugar una tarde... propondria otro juego. me van de un poco menos "peso" :D

RATING: 6
My head asplode.  I love the deductive component of this game, blending ingredients and trying to identify combinations and publish theories.  I don't like the rulebook and I don't like some of the euro elements.  The sell potion component in particular is OVERLY complicated. Debunking and conflict are very nebulous and I wonder if the conflict piece was to cover some hole in the mechanics.  This game has an apprentice level and master level and I think I'm going back to apprentice level.
Regarding the rulebook, they don't lead with the object of the game(how to win) which for a heavy game is important to know as you try to absorb the mechanical rules.  Also, the rulebook is full of fluff narration-type text which again in a heavy game is just annoying.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
The game is very hard to teach, takes up to an hour to do it. Maybe better if everybody playing has read the rulebook beforehand???

The game is beautiful, and components atleast OK. The deduction-sheets are crap, but fortunally there are better ones downloadable here on BGG.

Really like how they have integrated the deduction in this game with the other parts of it, and it doesn't feel like any other game I have played. Unique, mostly streamlined and very thematic!

Overall a good game, and after 3 plays I just want to explore the possibilities in this game more...

Update: 
After three plays in two days, I'm lowering my score to 8.5. This is because I know of no other game that I have played that has so many small extra rules, and every time I play, there is something new I discover (only three cubes first round, only first player to exhibit a specific potion in the exhibition gets a point for it etc etc...)

Update after 10 plays:
Never played the Master variant, as the debunking there seems to be hard to wrap your head around. For a game of its length, there is quite a bit of luck in it, which is unfortunate, but tolerable. No downtime to speak of, because when an other player takes a long time, you can take that time to check the boardstate and work on your deduction-grid. 
A heavy game in quite a few regards, but to be honest it is not really very deep. It most certainally works as a game, but definetly not for everyone...

RATING: 7
Very impressive and quality design. It's just very difficult to teach. The rules for alchemy take a good bit of explaining. The game can be punishing and it's absolutely brutal if you make an error in either your bookkeeping or logic, since early mistakes become the foundation for later deductions and cascade. Still, I feel like this could be a fantastic game if everyone is willing to suffer through 2-3 learning games first. Because most people won't be, I have to begrudgingly drop the rating for this one.

第II、III級正面朝上展開放置
3.原料卡洗混放置右下角，開5張出來、
***4.洗混探索者版塊一疊，隨機移除一個，第一張面朝上開出來，
會議版塊使用選擇的模式那面(大師 學徒)
一個放在兩個探險者後(第三張)
一個放在最後一張探險者前(倒數第二張)
***5.每位玩家起始2元，行動方塊是玩家(6-5-4 for 2-3-4 p)，
第一回合都使用三顆，抽兩張幫助卡棄一張，
原料卡學徒模式抽三張，大師抽兩張，
拿走一個顏色的獎牌，起始分數在10分
**6.除了卡牌內容(原料卡、幫助卡、勳章、特價卡)跟
推理內容其他都是公開的，神器卡不算。
7.理論版放置贊助板塊以及一個計分指示物，
藥性指示物放置一旁，鬥爭指示物大師模式才會使用。
8.使用APP共同輸入密碼。
遊戲進行:
1.遊戲總共進行六輪
A.由起始玩家開始輪流放順位軌並拿對應卡片或者幫助卡，
選擇第一個須付1元，抽卡都是從牌庫抽，之後順位照順位軌，
回合結束起始玩家標記向左傳(若玩家麻痺，則起始繼續左傳)
B.反順位軌放置行動方塊，一次全部放完，每人一排，從最下面開始放
C.所有行動從右下角開始順時鐘結算，每個行動先照順位作，在到第二排
**若反悔行動或不做行動可以將方塊移動到未使用區，
每兩個方塊可以抽一張幫助卡
行動說明:
1.採收原料:抽一張卡片，可拿翻開的或盲抽，不會馬上補完，全部做完後剩下的移除再補五張
2.提煉金錢:一張原料卡換取一枚金幣(蓋住棄牌)
3.購買神器卡:卡片左上角為所需支付金幣，右上角回最後額外分數，只有在第三輪會議結束跟第五輪會議結束會清掉換下個時代的神器。
4.實驗: 選兩個原料卡並用APP看結果，結果要公開，並放兩個對應的符號，一個到自己的公開圖版，原料卡蓋住棄至棄牌區，另外一個在自己實驗室圖板標示
**學徒試藥免費直到第一個出現負藥時之後都要付一塊錢
**自己試藥  若副作用藍色(失智藥水)扣一分聲望，綠色藥水(麻痺作用)順位軌變最後(多人照放進來的順位，起始玩家標記當輪跳過此人往左傳)，紅色藥水(免疫變弱進醫院)下輪該方塊移置未使用區，玩家少一個方塊
----------第一回合僅開放動作以上行動--------------
5.賣藥水:每回合一開始新的探險者會出現，決定賣的藥水種類(需兩個方塊)。
****賣藥水順序(若有多人要賣藥水)
在開始賣藥水前先用特價格決定賣藥水順位，每個人祕密出一張特價卡卡，如果平手就照原順位，決定順位是看特價卡上笑臉總和加上目前的聲望分數所提供笑臉(笑臉愈多代表賣給客人折扣越多，他越開心但你賺比較少)。
****聲望分數:
綠色區域(14~17分)加一笑臉，扣分也會多扣一分
藍色區域(18分以上)可多賺一元，扣分多扣兩分，
紅色區域(6分以下)少賺一元，扣分少扣一分
決定順序後，輪到你賣藥水時執行以下兩步驟
步驟一、先的人先選擇要賣哪個符號給冒險者(只能賣人數減一個，三人時，前兩個一組)，將其中一個方塊放置到該藥水下方空格，若無空位賣出則將方塊移置到未使用區。
步驟二、再選擇要配到什麼藥水品質程度，將另一個方塊移到標示上(每位玩家可重複採)
a.完全正確(顏色、符號正確)=>4元
b.＋－正確(顏色不對、符號正確)=>3元
c.中性藥水(中和標記)=>2元+扣1分
d.錯誤藥水(顏色、符號不正確)=>1元+扣一分
賣藥水跟試驗一樣，根據結果拿對應標記放在自己圖版 
**若你選擇C屬性但是配出AB其中一種只會得到2元不會扣分
6.發表論文:
 a.新論文:選擇一個成分標記放在一個元素格上，並放自己的獎章面朝下放在最上面，付一元且得一分。
b.支持別人的論文: 放獎章在該格下方並付一元給銀行，一元給前面的獎章所有人，不會得到一分。
*分數獎章猜對遊戲結束有分，元素獎章如果顏色對，猜錯不會扣分
***最先完成贊助版塊中兩個以上的發表的人可以拿走該版塊跟對應金幣，之後要完成其他贊助版塊就要三個都有發表才行，同時完成兩個的只能選一個拿。
7.反駁論文:
用APP反駁學徒模式只要指出一個元素不對即可，成功反駁加兩分，失敗扣一分。
****被反駁的如果獎章使用顏色跟反駁色相同不扣分，其他扣五分(不同色及星星計分)，所有獎章移出遊戲(反駁自己論文分數總和在加減)
****如果反駁成功且有行動在發表論文(下一格行動)可以馬上發表，但要是你反駁的元素或是藥性組成(可以將藥性組成移到正確元素上)，該行動方塊使用掉。
*大師模式必須指出哪兩種原料回合成出指定的符號，不管反駁幾個都是兩分，被扣分者若被扣10分只會算一次，如果發現兩個結果衝突，放衝突版塊在兩個元素上，且上面的獎章不能拿贊助，此兩元素也不能在被支持，直到其中一個被拿掉則衝突標記就移除
***一輪結束 
獎章發布最多的得一分，平手都一分(有紛爭標記獎章不算)
未使用區行動方塊每兩個抽一張幫助卡 
移動醫院內的方塊到未使用區
移除原料卡 補上新的5張
移除使用的探險者，開著的那張移過去並開新的
****如果是研習會議版塊，移到對應位置，下輪結束會開會議，並開新的探險者版塊，如果有開會議就跟據卡片上獎章數量加減分(有紛爭的獎章不算)
***會議結束放新的神器卡，之後起始標記換手開始新一輪
***最後一輪開始時放展覽會版塊取代測試藥位置
展覽行動更測試藥一樣，只是變成決定要你產生出什麼藥，成功加一分並放在大拇指向上的區域，只有第一個有分，如果該顏色兩種都產生就放在下面，加兩分，結束後一就會進行獎章最多的加分跟抽幫助卡並進入最後記分
*最後記分
1.所有名譽分就變成分數
2.加總神器分(魔鏡先算分)
3.加贊助分
4.幫助卡換兩塊錢，三塊一分
5.獎章根據結果記分，錯誤的扣四分(顏色獎章如果錯誤跟顏色一樣不扣分，超過兩個以上錯誤就扣分)，扣分跟顏色區域的額外扣分無關了
平手比錢

RATING: 5.5
After my first game: Nice production quality (artwork, sturdy cardboard), but the fiddly bits are also a huge downside in my opinion. The screens are rather high, you constantly have to reach around or over to access the main board without throwing your bits around. Teaching the rules takes rather long, gameplay drags on for quite a while, too. I prefer games that are easy to start and show their depth and complexity during play. Alchemists boiled down to learning a number of rules, drawing cards (element of randomness/ luck) and some deduction. During the game, many choices appeared that were not particularly interesting since I either had no way of determining which would be better, or the best choice was pretty clear right away, devaluing the decision-making. Lots of ballyho, but mediocre gaming experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Has Vlaada Chvatil been using an alias of Matuš Kotry lately? :)

I don't like deduction games and if the deduction was all there is in Alchemists I wouldn't have valued the game highly and I would've got rid of it right away.  Fortunately there is more to it than just figuring out how to link 8 potions' ingredients (feather, toad etc.) with 8 particles (so called 'alchemicals').
There is a whole layer of euro-game out there: a competition for order of play and a fierce struggle for profitable spots on the board (this part reminds me Dungeon Petz) like the ones which allow you to perform lucrative sales to customers (and earn scarce gold) and buy artifacts.   
You gain VPs from different sources (publishing/debunking theories based on your deduction (or guesses :) ), artifacts, grants, conferences, exhibition at the end etc).  So – as you can see – there are many aspects of the game into which the deduction is merely (but skillfully) interwoven.  Still, one could argue that everything revolves – more or less remotely - around the results of deduction (or guesses :) ) anyway.  But these other aspects are interesting by themselves and make the game a siginificantly different experience than a usual deduction game.

An innovative, fresh and satisfying design.  Exactly as Chvatil would've done it ;)

RATING: 9
This is one of my earliest Euros when I got back into boardgaming in 2015. I remember watching Rahdo's playthrough and so surprised at how the app works for this game. And the whole deduction part of the game works so well for my gaming group back then since we're more analytical players. We played this thrice within the week I bought it and this game quickly solidifies itself as my earlier favourites. 

The game in its core is a worker placement game. But throughout the game you're playing a mini game of discovering alchemical properties of 8 ingredients. That keeps players who "get the game" engaged since we're always wanting to find out what's the chemical components of all the ingredients. This works particularly well for an academic like me since its doing what I do in real life - publishing theories and debunking each others while working in the grand scheme of discovering the truth in each ingredients. Brilliant game.

Formula:

RATING: 3
Wow did I hate this game. Honestly based on my memory of the game the 4.5 I gave it seems pretty generous. My major problem with the game is the level of randomness. It's not necessarily the case that there's a lot of randomness but it has this odd dynamic where a small difference in outcomes can lead to a huge difference in points. And then this compounds, where if you fall behind you have to take bigger risks to get back in and then when those most likely fail, you're even farther behind.

It also has this odd feel where making some early guesses on the deduction before you've figured everything out is pretty heavily incentivised, but then you're in the dumb position of standing to either gain or lose a ton of points if you happened to guess right.

Though I guess they needed this system in place because the actual deduction is incredibly simple and so the challenge of the game can't come from there. I think I had everything figured out by about 2/3 of the way through the game, but then whether I could do anything useful with that came down to how well the adventurer happened to match with the ingredient cards that were available (or as was often the case, were top-decked).

So overall I think I'd say that this is a game with a really weak deduction system bolted on to an incredibly half-baked eurogame. Awful. I have no idea how this game isn't ranked in the thousands.

RATING: 2
I've only played once and I intend to keep it that way.  

The ipad mechanic is awful.  It slows down what would already be a drawn-out game (and that was just 3-player). 

There might be a mediocre-to-dull worker placement in there, but the components are a major barrier to playing.  They may look exciting, but trying to balance oversized cardboard counters up against undersized holes led to the inevitable clumsy hands (plus earth gravity and cardboard's low static adhesion properties) knocking them all on the table.  I couldn't remember where most of them went, but by that stage it really didn't matter.

The game taught me that I have difficulty distinguishing between slightly different shades of pastel colours, and difficulty recognising the difference between white plus and minus signs on a pale pink background.  Luckily those are skills I've never needed before, and if I avoid this game, I'll never need again.

RATING: 8.5
2015-03-06

Initial Rating: 7.5 (March 2014)

I was expecting a very good game from CGE and Alchemists doesn’t disappoint. Deduction is one of my favorite game mechanism and it is very well implemented in the game. 

The art is great, from the pretty player boards, to the colorful game board, the art really sells the theme. It has some resemblance to Dungeon Lords/Petz but that’s logical as all three games were drawn by the same artist (David Cochard). The game components are awesome, great quality cardboard.

When the game was explained to me, I thought it was a Vlaada game (but it is not :D), Alchemists has a similar feeling to some of his games.

The theme is great and the game mechanics do support it which is a rarity in an euro game.

Alchemists can be explained under 25 minutes and play time is around 2 hours. I have only played the apprentice version, but I really liked it. It has easy rules (lots of them), but the gameplay can be deep (medium weight) and it offers lots of interesting decisions each turn (which order of turn should I choose? Where to put my workers? Should I try the potion with my apprentice or with me? What ingredients should I mix?) with very limited resources so it has lots of tension. Money is very scarce and all the important actions require it. In particular I like to test ingredients so I can deduce what their composition, when you test there is a lot of tension in the table, I love it.

I also liked the game app which is definitively not a gimmick. We played it with an iPhone and 2 iPads and I have to say the pattern recognition seems to work better on the phone but it works very well on both devices. On the other hand, I really dislike that it is VERY easy for someone to cheat as there is a button in the app to reveal all the relevant info. Using the app for the game is mandatory, I know you could do without it, but then the game would be diminished. Another advantage the app has is that the game no longer can be broken due to a procedural human mistake which is a weakness most deduction games have. In Black Vienna if you give wrong information the game breaks, in Tragedy Looper if the Mastermind forgets to do a mandatory effect the game breaks, etc.

On the negative side, some artifacts seem overpowered, in particular the one that lets you retain an ingredient and the other that lets you peek at one ingredient of your opponents, not sure if that’s the case but I will play more to verify. It also has some luck of the draw with the ingredients but it doesn’t bother me yet.

Bottom line, Alchemists is a very good deduction and innovative game and one of the best 2014 games I have played so far. I’m glad to have it in my game collection. I can’t wait to play it again with my girlfriend who also loves deduction games. I’m also eager to see more games from Matus Kotry.

Current Rating: 7.5

RATING: 10
At first glance this appears to be a logic puzzle, and it certainly has that. But that's not the game. It also has what appears to be a gimmick, a smartphone app.  But it's implemented very well, and merely lets the game work without a GM.

The logic puzzle is enough to scare a lot of potential players off.  But if you get your head around it there's a whole game built on top. 

This game is a parody of Academia. You are supposed to be on a search to identify the 8 alchemical reagents in the game, but what you really do is race to publish your findings, ideally before you're even sure yourself. If your peers make a misstep, you can score a few points by rubbing their noses in it. And you make money by trading on your reputation, selling potions of dubious quality to transients.

-----------------------------------------------
Read rules Sept 2014

This is probably my most anticipated game of Essen '14. I really like deduction games and this one has a unique spin on the genre. Most deduction games have information on cards which the players see only some of. Additional information is gained by slowing seeing more cards and of course, deducing things! Alchemists uses an app (or web based program) to portion out the information. At first it may seem cosmetic, but it isn't. Accessing the hidden information in this way completely changes the type of deduction that is possible, and the dynamic between the players. Alice and Bob no longer have information you don't have, we all start the game in complete ignorance and must learn together. Of course, during the game we will learn different things.

The game is centered around 8 ingredients which are secretly mapped on to 8 'alchemicals'. Each alchemical has a red, blue, and green particle, each with a charge of either + or -. Hence there are 8 unique alchemicals. During the game you can mix two different ingredients together and the app tells you the result of the mixture. There are rules for how alchemicals react that I won't explain here. You privately note the results and make whatever deductions you can given the other experiments you've tried. Layered on top of this is an action selection system similar to worker placement. There are 7 different actions to choose from. Some help gather ingredients, some let you publish your theory of which alchemical a certain ingredient is. There are also special powers in the form of artifacts that you buy during the game.

It's a slightly complicated game. Medium, verging on medium-heavy. There are a lot of sources of victory points and ways to turn your deductions into winning. The art and presentation are top-notch, what we've come to expect from CGE. It's amazing the amount of excellent games they've published. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

To me, the gamey bits and pieces work thematically... and keep it all really interesting.  Not everyone's cup of tea... but I would play this anytime...

RATING: 7
The integrated app is a true innovation and the main reason for playing Alchemists. Testing ingredients creates an astonishing tension. Drawing the right deductions from the information is challenging. Mastering or even understanding the puzzle is difficult on first play. Add to this an enormous amount of upfront rules overhead as well as a long playing time (>3 hours) and there’s an high entry hurdle. To work out as a game in the long term, Alchemists has to prove that the deduction part remains challenging and not too luck-based while hopefully playing out in a shorter time with experienced players. Not sure though this will happen as the cult-of-the-new likely will slur over this game.

RATING: 7
I am really conflicted about this game.  I admired the design because I always thought it would be neat to have a deduction game where the deduction helps you toward winning but does not absolutely determine the winner.  I also enjoy the humorous commentary on the publish or perish nature of academia.  I was also rather obsessed with figuring how the deduction system work (mostly because I made some deduction mistakes in my first couple of games that I was eager to overcome).

However, the deduction element went from brain-melty in the first game to "solved" after playing around with the deduction system.  So what is left is the worker placement part of the game--while it is interesting, I don't know if it can carry the game.

The first couple of games took way too long (4 hours) because everyone was struggling with the deduction system.  This might be a reasonable game if it took an hour, but I don't know if the game can be brought to that length with experience.

RATING: 9
My only complaint is a possible lack of replayability - I'm not sure if the difference in the artifact breakout would be enough for me to stay interested ten or twenty games in.

Let me add that I'm one of the cranks who was skeptical about the app. Even though before playing this, I complained about having to download something, believe me, I don't think that aspect should affect whether you play or buy this game. The app works well, it's necessary, and it's not over-relied upon, either, and the game makes it waaaay worth it.

The deduction and strategy are both intense and completely intuitive. People might refer to this as a puzzle game like Mystery of the Abbey, but for my money, the incredible implementation and the surprisingly subtle analysis of how science works make it far more engaging. The gameplay, too. You aren't just asking questions; the game requires bluffing, double bluffing, keeping careful track of what you're revealing (or not), what your opponents are (or not)...

I really liked it. I think it's ripe for an expansion, though. I think the strategy is straightforward enough that if everybody just adopted the attitude that they're trying to win by finding out the truth and using deduction (rather than playing a social game), it would wear out its welcome, and that does seem like the best strategy, so...

RATING: 9
I like Alchemists. The way the Mastermind aspect interacts with the gameplay is really great. That said, it tends to go just SLIGHTLY longer than I like (most of my favorite games take 2 hours with setup, this takes 2 or more without counting setup). For being so long, I dislike how thoroughly a single mistake can screw you. Fail to recognize you're making an assumption somewhere? Prepare to lose ALL your points. Accidentally mix two ingredients you've already combined before? Prepare to fall hopelessly behind. Still, it's a great experience despite that.

Edit: Bumping this up to a 9. I see now that a single mistake doesn't screw you so badly as I thought, because you can get a TON of information just from other players' theories, even if they might not quite be right. Meaning you have somewhere to start from even if you bunk up your deductions. The increased pressure to publish in the advanced version of the game means more info flows into the game, and it is extremely fun!

RATING: N/A
In Alchemists, two to four budding alchemists compete to discover the secrets of their mystical art. Points can be earned in various ways, but most points are earned by publishing theories – correct theories, that is — and therein lies the problem.

The game is played in six rounds. At the beginning of the round, players choose their play order. Those who choose to play later get more rewards.
Players declare all their actions by placing cubes on the various action spaces, then each action space is evaluated in order. Players gain knowledge by mixing ingredients and testing the results using a smartphone app (iOS, Android, and also Windows) that randomizes the rules of alchemy for each new game. And if the alchemists are longing for something even more special, they can always buy magical artifacts to get an extra push. There are 9 of them (different for each game) and they are not only very powerful, but also very expensive. But money means nothing, when there's academic pride at stake! And the possession of these artifacts will definitely earn you some reputation too. Players can also earn money by selling potions of questionable quality to adventurers, but money is just a means to an end. The alchemists don't want riches, after all. They want respect, and respect usually comes from publishing theories.

RATING: 6.7
This is a Czech board game that feels in many ways like Dungeon Lords – funny fantasy theme, overcrowded board with somewhat fun illustrations, some worker placement-ish (but not really wp) mechanism, some heavy puzzle-solving and lots of fiddly rule details. The latter made it less enjoyable for me even though the idea is interesting – mix components to see the result of the mixes (trying them on an apprentice or yourself – bearing the consequences) ; make assumptions about the components’ attributes based on the results and get famous if you are successful. My only problem is that from the start of the rule explanation to the end of the game 4 hours passed – so even though I quite liked it, I’m not sure I want to try it again.
