=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Gran juego. No tiene demasiada interacción directa entre jugadores, sino que se trata de planear una estrategia con un ojo en tu zona de juego mientras que con el otro intentas averiguar, y si es posible echar por tierra, las de tus rivales al tiempo que evitas que ellos hagan lo propio contigo.
Hay que estar muy concentrado durante la partida pues hay que tener en cuenta bastantes factores antes de poder elegir la estrategia a seguir. Cualquier distracción o despiste puede echar al traste una ronda de juego (tiene 6). No es un juego rápido de jugar, pero sí muy inmmersivo y adictivo.

A la hora de crear pociones es conveniente seguir un método: realizar pruebas consecutivas donde al menos una de las cartas se repita permite descubrir con relativa prontitud qué especie alquímica está asociada a qué ingrediente. De esa manera se podrán proponer teorías certeras que nos darán PV al final de la partida. 

Proponer a sabiendas teorías incorrectas a modo de cebo para que otros jugadores las refrenden y pierdan PV al final de la partida es otro de los aspectos que me han gustado mucho.

La fase de vender pociones aporta un aliciente añadido al juego, pues entran en juego unas cartas que pueden modificar el orden de juego. No todos los jugadores pueden vender pociones en la misma ronda, luego las peleas por ser el afortunado que consiga vender las preciadas pociones están aseguradas, ya que es la mejor manera de conseguir Oro, muy necesario en el juego: comprar artefactos, crear/refrendar teorías/ crear pociones...

Es un juego lleno de posibilidades donde cada partida es diferente gracias  a la aplicación del móvil, todo un hallazgo.


Nota: 
Q dice que no le gusta que una teoría propuesta por un jugador pueda ser utilizada por el resto de jugadores para puntuar refrendando esa teoría. Cierto, pero el jugador que propone una nueva teoría disfruta de beneficios exclusivos.
Creo que el hecho de poder refrendar una teoría ajena no afea en absoluto la mecánica del juego, pues se pueden proponer teorías incorrectas a propósito para engañar a los otros jugadores.Además, el jugador que propone una teoría puede estar equivocado y arrastrar involuntariamente en su error al resto de jugadores que hayan refrendado su teoría, lo que puede dar sorpresas inesperadas al final de la partida.

El juego funciona muy bien con 2 jugadores, pues permite pensar estrategias a corto/medio plazo. Con más jugadores aumentan las variables a tener en cuenta y por tanto se pierde control. En estos casos, la mejor opción consiste en ser flexible y aprovechar de la mejor manera posible la situación cambiante de la partida en cada ronda.

Incorporar mayor cantidad de cartas de guerreros y de artefactos habría aumentado todavía más la rejugabilidad, lo que haría subir de nota al juego. Quizá posibles expansiones del juego solucionen este punto negativo.

RATING: 8
Sept 2015:

Having only played this game twice I'm giving this a initial rating of 8. It's a bit of a bear to teach timewise, but once the game has been grokked it's surprisingly simple.

There are so many ways to deduce the various alchemistry of the ingredients and that alone gives this game a very high replayability. Add on a very solid and rather tight worker-placement game and you've got a dynamic experience.

I think this game has an excellent narrative throughout the gameplay and there is a satisfaction as it wraps up.

The app is perfect and handles the load of randomizing the keeping everything smooth and honest. I'm looking forward to seeing where games go with app inclusion from here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A very unique game with its deduction and innovative with its use of an app to facilitate it. This game's theme feels as much about academia as it does about magic (in a good way), as the whole publishing of results and debunking feels a lot like research papers. 

I would say that there aren't actually that many rules in this game, it's just how it works is so different from anything I've experienced that makes it so difficult to grasp. 

This game is more to me an action point allowance game than a worker placement, because you can't block people off a spot usually, it's just that turn order can be really important in this game.

The rules say not to mention the debunking, publishing, and the selling potions at the start of the game, but I would mention that the potions are a really easy way to get gold. You can actually get a lot of deduction from seeing what other people debunk rather than performing your own research, or sometimes even guessing when you are selling potions. Note that since you can only score at most 5 of the ingredients (besides the grants) getting points for reputation can also be quite important.

EDIT: The novelty of the app has worn off a bit and I dislike how much you are incentivized to do 50/50 guesses in the beginning of the game to get the bonus scoring and the grants. This takes away from the importance of deduction in this game, and while I don't mind 50/50 guesses as much for games such as Tragedy Looper, for a heavy Euro that is not ideal. I'm not sure exactly what to rate this, I made a major mistake that really screwed me over and had a terrible time because of it, but was already seeing I would not enjoy this game as one I rated an "8" beforehand.

EDIT 2: While the app is really well integrated I dislike how you can for the most part play this as a straight Euro, especially the need to "publish" early just to score points. That really pulls away from the deduction of the game (which also is starting to feel a little bit samey) and the feel of discovering a new element, and makes it feel much more ingrained in the lows of academia. Overall innovative, but once the wow factor wears off not as much fun for a person who likes his Euros less dry.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Score is for 2-player game, and it seems like it'd be a solid 9 or higher with 3-4 players. Super cool deduction game with nice component quality. Overall it has a great blend of game mechanics (primarily deduction and "worker placement") so that you always feel like you're doing something meaningful. 

Pros: Unique deduction design that feels engaging and exciting, tight gameplay so that each turn feels like it really matters, minimal randomness with solid avenues to mitigate any randomness that does come up. 

Cons: Very steep learning curve/drawn out explanation process -- it takes a minute to pick up the "potion-making" pattern and even after that you have to explain the overall gameplay with lengthy descriptions of each action & its specific rules/exceptions/etc. It plays smoothly once you get it, but the overall "teaching" process to new players takes forever. Also, it's somewhat lengthy; it does depend on familiarity with the game & number of players (and honestly it feels like it goes fast) but you should expect at least 1.5hrs. We took 2 hours to finish our first 2player play-through. 

I'm eager to play with 4 people as I expect it'll be more cut-throat and challenging to take advantages with the added competition. I could potentially see the game losing its enjoyability if you played it super often (unlike games like, say, Codenames that are built for replayability) but it seems to have enough variation built-in that it'd last you a long time if you played semi-often and forever if you play less often. 

8.5/10!

RATING: N/A
Neat little game, but requires a free app. Kind of trial and error thing where you combine two things and the app shuffles what the recipes result in. You're testing the potions on students, paying for things, there's a little worker placement, and trying to make money by the end, including a little convention thing at the last round where you try and prove you've come up with a potion. Can have really funny results as things fail, and a neat little logic puzzle, and since it's shuffled and never the same. You also can "publish" recipes, and get prestige, but you can often do so without entirely being sure. Also more about the qualities of the ingredients rather than strickly X + Y = Z.

RATING: 7.5
Really unique game. Not for everyone, however. At its core, this is a deduction game. Everything you do is geared toward deducing the two agents (called alchemicals) that make up each potion. 
I personally like this one a lot, but even I recognize there’s crunchiness here that is both good and bad. I think the alchemical concept could have been done better. If you can’t eventually wrap your brain around it, you’ll probably be too frustrated to appreciate the game’s other shining features, such as the testing on yourself or a student, and the place last, act first worker placement mechanic. 
So this one’s like a Terry Pratchett novel: has a lot of talent in its making, but some people are just not gonna dig it. 
I personally like it very much.

Normally when I don't like the game, I just move on and leave it behind. In this case however I can't.
How can this be, that such a mess is rated SO high. I might don't like all the games from the top of the ranking but it's due to personal preferences. Here however, the game is just weak.

- Luck dependent.

RATING: 8
My wife loves this game! This is probably the heaviest game that she is willing to play with me as she tends to like cute, lighter games such as Takenoko and Sushi Go! I'm pretty sure its the cat on the box...

I also really like this game, especially the deductive nature of finding the alchemical symbols. The app used with this game is super handy and fun. I also enjoy how much strategy the game offers with worker placement, especially in the later rounds. The game also has a small push-your-luck aspect when testing ingredients on yourself, or when you try to sell a potion off that you're not quite sure is what the buyer is looking for. I've often found that the Debunk Theory spaces are not used often as most players I've played with try to wait until they are sure of the ingredients prior to publishing.

The only thing I don't like about the game is teaching it, especially to people who don't play many heavy board games and can't wrap their heads around how the deduction works. Its a minor set back though, it just means I will only play this game with specific people which really is to be expected for this type of game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
FIRST THOUGHTS: I quite like this one, though it feels (in its base form; have only played the expansion once) a little underbaked in spots. If players accidentally stumble in rounds 2-3 on the correct alchemical for several ingredients, the solving portion of the game becomes very dull, and it turns into a worker-placement/optimization game. [This is changed in the expansion of course.] -- This is a game where player count makes a huge difference: in 2-player or 3-player games there is less scrutiny of theories because there are simply fewer eyes on them/cubes for publishing and testing, so the chance of last-minute reversals is much greater; in 4-player games anything erroneous is likely to get debunked quickly. -- That said, it's a fascinating puzzle at times and I like games which reward risky play rather than safe play: taking a chance on selling an unknown potion or publishing an early half-proven theory is often better in the long run than boringly waiting to nail the facts down, and you can also just play to accumulate money and artifacts and swoop in at the end to drop 5- and 3-point ribbons on theories that other players have worked so hard on.

FURTHER THOUGHTS: 2-player is kind of frustrating: given how essential money is in this game, it's vital to claim the Sell Potion spot every round: either sell a potion or offer such a steep discount that the other player has to offer -2 or -3 likewise just to sell one, and then you get a free favour card for your trouble. You can't afford to let the other player sell a potion for 3 or 4 gold (or maybe 5 in later rounds!!). -- So there's a forced move EVERY ROUND which is problematic in terms of game design. Am I missing something? 3-player seems ideal.

Also: one key to playing this game is to watch other players' moves carefully, and hide your own as much as possible. Try to avoid letting people know too much about the cards in your hand (by claiming ingredients via the turn order track or drawing blindly from the deck). Otherwise sharp-eyed players can make free deductions based on observing your actions. (e.g. if you let your hand go down to 1 card, then grab Scorpion and sell a potion successfully, then other players know something about Scorpion without going to the trouble of a test.)

The artifacts are.... interesting. Initially the Boots of Speed, Robe of Respect and Magic Mortar seem terribly overpowered but I've found that on the whole you can win without any of them. Grab them if you can, don't kill yourself if you can't (but don't let someone pig out on artifacts if Crystal Cabinet is coming out later).

MORE THOUGHTS: I play this mostly online; playing it in person, it's clear to me that playing this well requires you to engage as fully with the social deduction game as with the pure deduction game. Track the ingredients players are snapping up, the potions they know how to make, and it's easy to get a ton of information without having to pay for it with ingredients or money or action cubes. Take a spare sheet and in addition to marking down theories, mark down how many ingredients players have at the start and end of rounds--you can then deduce what experiments they are doing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is a great heavy euro, with in my opinion the best app integration in board games. The app is not intrusive, easy to use and very intuitive. The game mechanics itself is not heavy, but the combination of the action spaces where you mix the potions and deduction element (finding out which ingredient matches which alchemicals) makes for a very challenging experience. There is an aspect to pushing your luck with the publication actions, but hedging  against the correct colour would be wise.

The only negative I have about the game is maybe the playtime.It is not that we felt that it was too long, but 2 hour games mean it might not get to the table as often. This is also why it is in my opinion best with two.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
Well, this is an interesting game. The deduction portion is sort of like a "game within the game", as it took some time to understand how that part works separately from the rest of the game itself.

It's a fantastic game. Of course, the deduction is the star of the show. But what makes the game really interesting is attempting to infer information from what your opponents are doing. For example: my wife published a theory on scorpion. How confident am I that she actually knows all 3 ingredients? If I am confident, then I can endorse that theory (for points) and perhaps infer information about other ingredients. But of course, she could publish a theory where she only knows 2 of the 3 ingredients, knowing that may cause me to make an incorrect reference if I believe she actually knows all 3. So while the interaction in the game is indirect, it's always present and something one needs to think about.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
What a game! There are two parts to this game: the Mastermind style puzzle and the Worker Placement actions. Both parts mesh together really well giving the feel of a single gaming experience.
After a false start where I had misplaced a token on my board and then re-starting the game, I thoroughly enjoyed it. I think that you need to play this in a relaxed manner where if someone says they need 5 minutes to consider their next move, then let them. Trying to hurry the game along will ruin it. That said the 2 player game ran in around 2.5 hours.
My concern with the game is that if you make a mistake with deductions, you can start with a new sheet and rework all your logic, but if you put a token in the wrong position it is unrecoverable. So place carefully!
Great game, and I am looking forward to my next session.
After playing a 3 player game where someone misplaced a token and trashed their game....the warning is all the more important.

RATING: 7
What a game! Every time I have opened this game I have had so much fun. From the absolutely sheer terror of finding out that mixing a crow's foot and a cane toad together creates a potion showing an aspect you already knew. To the thrill of disproving your friends published work exposing him/her as the fraud you know them to be.

This game creates tension though publishing academic works first, proving that you're the smartest alchemist in your gaming group. High risks have high rewards but even higher penalties. Being the smartest alchemist makes more, earns greater reputation and generally makes you look pretty tough among your peers. However errors are penalized at a greater extent, scrutiny and pressure bare down upon you as you try and impress your peers at the exhibition. Overall this is a delightful puzzle game with enough competition to get my laziest friend off the couch and joining our gaming group.

RATING: 8
This game has got mixed reviews but the mechanisms of deduction, bluffing and building on hidden information did appeal to me. The deduction game is simple once you understand how it works - simply mix ingredients to find their common elements until you're ready to deduce their composition. However, game is not only about mixing and deducing but rather about knowing when to take which actions to be ahead of the other players. This is particularly true when it comes to publishing your findings, where you can bluff and then debunk your own theory to earn reputation and, more importantly, confuse your opponents.

There are some aspects that concern me, like the lack of a rubber band mechanism, some potentially over-powered artefacts and an anticlimactic last round when (as it happened in my game at least) all compositions are known and the rest of the game is about maximizing points. It may also be too long for a game with so many random elements. Nevertheless, the game experience is fun and that's what counts!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The three-way balancing act between gambling, bluffing and information-gathering is the real hook of the game. Emergent gameplay seeps through every design choice, and the game is definitely not centered around deduction at the expense of other fun factors. One round you feel like being at a casino at Las Vegas, and the next round you feel like a mathematician in a chemistry laboratory -- but not at the game's mercy, but rather through your free will to choose. The 'crazy world of academia'-theme is very funny and realistic. I'd say that the thematic immersion is some of the best I've experienced (and I'm in academia in real life): the players really become one with their characters (more than mere role play) and get sucked into the twisted psychology of this universe. Components are beautiful, the elegant app works like a dream (and adds to immersion even more) and game length is perfectly optimized to tell a story of many surprise moments and surprise discoveries, from zero knowledge to complete mastery. Artwork and components are the best imaginable (except for color misalignment on the deduction board). The design is like an elegant mathematical proof crossbred with a street magic trick. Playing this game feels like living in a fairy tale and truly becoming part of it. Where else can you publish nonsensical theories, get fame for it, spend the money greedily, plan to debunk your own theory and then race with others to do it, guess in advance how a theory will be debunked when first 'supporting it' etc.? It is marvellous and a lot more intuitive than it sounds. Exciting times are guaranteed.

RATING: 6
Played a late stage prototype.  The concept and implementation with the app is brilliant, hands down.

It loses points for me because the marriage of logic puzzle with highly interactive worker placement strategy will naturally lend itself to significant analysis paralysis, and is not really avoidable if you want to play well.  A secondary concern is a fair amount of luck in what you can learn.  You are limited to learning about the aspects of the cards you have drawn, which won't always match up with what you would actually like to know.

Definitely try it for yourself - the experience is like no other game out there.  But for me it is a miss for the above reasons.

RATING: 2
Love the central deduction piece, but there's a bit too much fluff around it. If it could be winnowed to remove the need for money and the favor cards (thus removing the imbalancing aspects), it might play more quickly and be a Clue killer. Still a great game -- for the central deduction concept -- but the extra-ness of it is a turn off.
-----
There's just way too much bloat, and too much that hinges on guesses. To illustrate the latter: I could spend my first turn narrowing down an ingredient to one of two alchemicals. On the second turn, publish a theory about the ingredient, making a 50/50 guess. Now, do I hedge my bet with 100% safety, or go for the points? What I do here is a game-deciding move, so early on. If I am wrong, then hedging my bet provides 3 rewards -- I can debunk my own theory in the 3rd round for 2 points, I won't lose points in doing so, and I can re-publish the correct theory and get 5 points from it. OTOH, if I am right, then I must bet 5 points on the theory, or else not derive any gain from my research at the end of the game.  What a stupid non-dilemma. There's no strategy in the decision, just a wild guess. Or is the strategy to purposely publish the wrong theory and pursue avenue 1? Downgrading from 6.5.

RATING: 8
The art, CGE style, is very cool and the design is not great, but attractive for sure. The manual is fun and didactic, CGE style as well,.

The deduction part is not complicated, but at least is more complex than anything I have seen so far in boardgames. I guess this is so because otherwise, it could create huge differences between players. But the deduction is only a part of the game. The worker placement part is also very important. It starts with the initiative system from Fresco and takes it to another level creating an order of resolution in the actions themselves; this way, you might get penalized for not being first or even some action could become speculative.

The game is thinky (especially until you master the deduction part), but interesting and fun.

My biggest issue is that I have serious concerns about the replayability. The game works very well, and I'm sure it will keep working well forever, the games will be different, but once the deduction part becomes natural and effortless, I might not want to pay it again (or yes, who knows).

I'm not convinced by the refutation mechanic. Once the game is advances, it's very difficult to find the coloured tile you want, it will probably have been used for another element, and you night not have enough information to refute it.


Still, right now it's a breeze of fresh air in my boardgaming life.

So far, the best deduction game I've seen!

First impression [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/3737573#item3737573]here[/url]!

RATING: 4
Strong contender for Worst Usability of the Year. Microscopic icons, half of which have insufficient contrast. Tiny text on cards. Inefficient board layout. RATING: 10
I usually do enjoy logic puzzles and there are already a couple of games that use them as a game. This is however the first one where it feels to me like the puzzle is really integrated into an overall game and it's not just a puzzle with some sot of scoring added on top. 

In addition to this the theme is just what makes me really love this game. I work in research and so many of the mechanics just deal with this in a very ironic and almost satirical way, which gives it this additional charming thematic flavor. 

I guess for many people downtime can get a problem here (especially when people take a while to scan their cards), but to me it's just one of the games where I know it can take a little longer and if I'm up for this type of game, I don't mind it at all. It's just a game, where I'm aware it has this big logic puzzle and thinking about my own experiments, what I can deduce from them, which experiments I should do next, which theories of others I might be able to prove wrong, gives me enough to think about, while it's not my turn that I don't feel bored during downtime. 

And of course a comment on the app: I was actually more sceptical about it than excited at first and what draw me to the game was more the theme than the app. But I do think it is well integrated here. It's not just a gimmick but really makes the gameplay possible smoothly without needing a gamemaster and there is enough going on on the board and with the cards, cubes, tiles etc. that it doesn't reduce the feel of a boardgame for me. 

So overall really great game.

My son (12 years old) it's a great fan of this game, even though he can not yet manage correctly all the logic thinking involved in it. 
I, on the other hand, am not as keen as him towards it. I must say this is a game for advanced or at least experimented board gamers only, as it has a complexity of rules that can take you up to 3 hours to just pick up the game play. With that said, I thought I was left with very short amount of opportunities in it's rounds and when I finally was picking up the clues (as it will depend a lot on the card drawing) the game ended! It is a bitter taste you're left with when you've spend 3 hours trying to do something and because of one bad decision you are helpless and unable to pick yourself up.
I don't think I'll be playing this again, but for some reason this is a LOVE IT/ HATE IT kind of game and I'm definitely not on the Love-it side.

RATING: 8.9
Such an innovative game. Can seem somewhat fiddly the first few times you play it. But third go around really starts to shine. Hoping that the constant consulting of the rulebook diminishes and game speed picks up. But just love the theme of passive-agressive academics sniping each other. The game board art is really capitvating. It has that Vlaada Chvatil style rulebook with humorous asides. It actually is a great teaching aide by really infusing the rules with theme. For example you can test your potions on students. The first time someone poisons the student with a negative potion in the round his enthusiam for science greatly diminishes, you now have to pay him 1 gold to for him to agree to remain your
 guinea pig. If the rule was delivered in a dry no nonsense matter sure its easier to lookup later but I argue because of the fun and thematic flavor text you will never forget the rule in the first place.

RATING: 8.5
規則:
*原料總共只有八個可能，兩種混和會顯示一大一小一樣+或-的結果，完全相消的會顯示中性藥水
*洗混探索者版塊一疊，隨機移除一個，開第一張，會議版塊使用選擇的模式那面(大師 學徒)一個放在兩個探險者後 一個放在最後一張探險者前
*神器卡分等級各抽三張打開，I級的放在圖版上，剩下的收回盒中
*展覽板塊最後一回合取代吃藥水動作
*材料卡洗勻放在圖版上，開五張放下面
*除了卡牌內容跟推理內容其他都是公開的
*每人起始兩元，抽兩張幫助卡棄一張，原料卡學徒模式抽三張，大師抽兩張，行動方塊數量依玩家數目而訂(6-5-4 for 2-3-4 p)，第一回合都使用三顆，拿走一個顏色的獎牌，起始分數在10分
*遊戲進行六輪
A.起始玩家開始輪流放順位軌並拿對應卡片，抽卡都是從牌庫抽，之後順位照順位軌，然後起始玩家標記左傳
B.反順位軌放置行動方塊，一次全部放完，每人一排，從最下面開始放
C.所有行動從右下角開始順時鐘結算，每個行動第一個先照順位作，在到第二個
*選擇不做的行動可把行動方塊移到不使用區，回合結束每兩個可抽一張幫助卡
*行動列表
1.抽原料卡 抽一張，可拿開著的或盲抽，不會馬上補，全部做完後剩下的移除在補五張，用完棄牌堆洗勻形成新牌庫
2.轉換金幣 蓋著棄一張原料卡換一個金幣
3.買神器 只有在第三輪會議結束跟第五輪會議結束會清掉換下個時代的神器
4.實驗 選兩個原料並用APP看結果，結果要公開，並放一個對應的到自己圖版，原料卡蓋著棄
>學徒試藥免費直到第一個出現負藥時之後都要付一塊錢
>自己吃 副作用藍色扣一分，綠色順位軌最後(多人照放進來的順位，起始玩家標記當輪跳過此人往左傳)，紅色 下輪少一個方塊
*第二輪後可選行動如下
5.賣藥水 先的人先選擇要買哪個(只能賣人數減一個，三人時，前兩個一組)，全部選完在選擇要保證配到什麼程度(完全正確，+-正確，中性以上，隨便)>做藥水跟測試一樣，根據結果拿對應標記放在自己圖版 > 根據結果有沒有達到預期拿錢，下面兩級不管怎樣都要扣一分
*在開始賣藥水前先用特價格決定賣藥水順位，如果平手就照原順位，決定順位是看特價卡上笑臉總和加上目前的名譽分數，綠色區域加一笑臉，扣分也會多扣一分，藍色區域可多賺一元，扣分多扣兩分，紅色區域少賺一元，扣分少扣一分
6.發表論文 
>第一個發表，選擇一個成分標記放在一個元素格上，並放自己的獎章在最上面，付一元並得一分
>仰慕別人的論文，放獎章在該格並付一元給銀行，一元給前面的獎章所有人
*分數獎章猜對遊戲結束有分，顏色獎章如果錯的是該顏色，猜錯不會扣分
*最先完成贊助版塊中兩個以上的發表的人可以拿走該版塊跟對應金幣，之後要完成其他贊助版塊就要三個都有發表才行，同時完成兩個的只能選一個拿
7.反駁論文 用APP反駁學徒模式只要指出一個元素不對即可，成功加兩分，失敗扣一分，被反駁的如果獎章使用顏色跟反駁色相同不扣分，其他扣五分，所以獎章移出遊戲(反駁自己論文分數總和在加減)
*如果反駁成功且有行動在發表論文可以馬上發表，但是要是你反駁的元素或是組成，該行動方塊使用掉
*大師模式必須指出哪兩種原料回合成出指定的符號，不管反駁幾個都是兩分，如果發現兩個結果衝突，放衝突版塊在兩個元素上，且上面的獎章不能拿贊助，此兩元素也不能在被仰慕，直到其中一個被拿掉則衝突標記就移除
*一輪結束 獎章發布最多的得一分，平手都一分，未使用區行動方塊每兩個抽一張幫助卡，移動醫院的方塊到未使用區，移除使用的探險者，開著的那張移過去並開新的，如果是會議版塊，移到對應位置，下輪結束會開會議，並開新的探險者版塊，如果有開會議就跟據獎章數量加減分(有衝突的獎章不算)，會議結束放新的神器卡，之後起始標記換手開始新輪
*最後一輪開始時放展覽會版塊取代測試藥位置，展覽行動跟測試藥一樣，只是變成決定要你產生出什麼藥，成功加一分並放在大拇指向上的區域，不是第一個沒分，如果該顏色兩種都產生就放在下面，加兩分，之後進行獎章最多的加分跟抽幫助卡並進入最後記分
*最後記分
1.所有名譽分就變成分數
2.加總神器分(魔鏡先算分)
3.加贊助分
4.幫助卡換兩塊錢，三塊一分
5.獎章根據結果記分，錯誤的扣四分(顏色獎章如果錯誤跟顏色一樣不扣分，超過兩個以上錯誤就扣分)，扣分跟顏色區域的額外扣分無關了
平手比錢

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Alchemists is one of the first app-integrated games, which makes potion-making easier for everyone. And the game is atmospheric and gorgeous to look at, too. It's one of those perfect games where theme meets mechanics effortlessly. The basic concept of the game is Clue with worker placement: there are eight mysterious ingredients with magical properties to be solved by the end of the game. Players gather then mix the ingredients to make potions with positive or negative effects. With the results, players can then trace back which properties belong to which ingredients. But don't be tricked into thinking that you automatically win by solving all eight ingredients; it's just a distraction. The reality is that the player who best utilizes their limited but correct information about some of the ingredients is the winner, by making a claim on a particular ingredient, endorsing another player's claim or debunking false claims. At it's heart, Alchemists is a victory-point-driven Euro with inventive mechanics and it's balanced for different strategies. The board, components, and overall design of the game are beautiful but set-up and teaching can be a bear. Luckily, turns flow nicely and are intuitive. Don't be fooled by the box art and cute rules! There is a lot of strategy in Alchemists!

Third, the deductive nature of the game seems to be fairly straightforward.  On our first play, we were all too tentative to bluff or to challenge others for bluffing, and I misread my own notes to where I thought someone else was bluffing only to discover I agreed with them completely.  We did not race to publish, and I am sure that once you see the need to do so, it gives the game an extra dynamic.

When our game ended, I really wanted to set it up and play again immediately, but did not have the time to do so.

RATING: 7
Alchemists is a competitive deduction game in which players are trying to figure out the composition of various alchemical elements based on the effects they produce when mixed together. The game uses a mobile app to randomize the setup and administrate the game (alternatively, a non-playing person can do this). Combined elements (called “potions”) can be tested on students (who may charge you), yourself (which may have very adverse effects), or customers (who cost more action points and may either give you money or cost you points). Either way, the resultant potion will have a color and a sign, which can be marked in your screen. Once you have enough information about a given element, you can choose to publish your findings for victory points. There are incentives to publish before your information is complete, but if you’re wrong then other players can choose to debunk your research which gives them points and costs you points. On top of this game lies an action selection system that’s very reminiscent of Dungeon Lords or Dungeon Petz. I enjoyed the deduction elements of this game, but some of the chrome didn’t sit well with me--the publishing system had a “get there first” kind of feel, and action selection mechanism seemed a little overwrought and fiddly.

(2 plays)

RATING: 7
Quite the interesting game. It's part deduction and part worker placement. I do find the worker placement probably the weakest part of the game, but the deduction is quite fun. The worker placement part of the game just felt slow and a bit strange at times. Almost clunky.

The rulebook was both amazingly wonderful and horrible at the same time. I found a lot of useful information in learning the alchemical properties and deducing things, but a few spots the rules seem quite vague. The first game also blew my mind in terms of trying to solve things. I think I have a pretty good grasp of it now and I'm still quite enthralled with it. I fucked up big time my first game in crossing off things and we ended up with only partially correct theories. 

A solid deduction game even if it's a bit slow in spots.

Side note, but I was [i]this[/i] close to tearing one of the cauldron shelves off. I mangled the red one slightly thinking that I was supposed to be punching it out and had a shitty cut. The rules don't mention this anywhere and I was glad I stopped where I did.

[b]Update[/b]: I worry that I might be too good at this game. I was able to solve most of the alchemical properties within the first few turns and if my opposing player was correct, I was able to knock them all down to correct answers within those few turns. Not sure what I can do to hinder that. I suppose I did get slightly "lucky" with testing a few potions on myself, but more often than not, I was able to reduce down things fairly easily. Still a fun game, but I'm worried that I'll crush a new player.

I'm also unsure of how better to publish theories. I was correct early and often and I had no issue hedging bets against one that I thought might be wrong or I knew was wrong only to debunk it and republish it later. I wonder if more players will force me to strategize differently, so I look forward to a 3 or 4 player game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is a deduction game that uses an app to randomize the answers you are looking for with each play. It's an interesting idea, and I didn't mind the app being part of the game design. The issue I had was that the game, outside of the goal of deducing the potion results is very finicky with a lot to remember. There's not much time or ability to deduce, and the things that punish you most are like, you forgot you needed 1 gold, or you forgot you needed a worker in two different spots to do this action/combo thing. 

There is also some "double dick-punching", where if you publish something wrong, which you have to do unless you are nuts lucky, then someone can knock you off, and you lose a ton of points, and THEN take more damage from a round event that hurts you if you don't have X published theories. 

I liked the deduction part, but by the end the other rules frazzled me that I mixed a invalid potion that I already knew how to make because I got the ingredient colors messed up. I had set the correct cards I needed to mix the potion in front of me so I wouldn't forget, and then pulled two wrong cards from my hand to make the potion and failed. This of course isn't the game's fault, but my point is just that the game requires you to remember a lot of stuff that isn't related to the deduction part of the game... and the deduction part of the game, you actually barely make progress on because of lack of available actions and a lot of dependency on luck.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:nostar: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 7.4
The deduction puzzle that's central to this game is absolutely fantastic, and I love the mad scramble towards publishing one's theories - often having to make the terrible decision between expediency or accuracy.

However, the euro part of this euro game is lacking.  The worker placement element is clunky and unintuitive, which when combined with inconsistent iconography and a visually noisy game board makes the game a bear to teach. 
Also, I feel that having the availability of basic resources be random in a competitive logic puzzle is poor design.

If Alchemists received a second edition that streamlined its iconography and board mechanics, I'd buy it in a heartbeat.

RATING: 10
My current addiction, it's such a fun and satisfying game. The deduction becomes straightforward after a few games but that just opens you up to the capacity for inference. Red picked up a toad, did he use it this turn? Did he use two toads? Is he holding it to mess with you? Green says mushroom is +++, do you trust her enough to use that information in your grid? 

The worker placement component is IMO as robust as any other non-Uwe game. I have to put my cubes down before Blue, will he snatch that sweet item I want? Will he debunk Red's preposterous theory of fern before I get a chance to rub his nose in it? 

I've seen some people complain that there aren't enough actions. Your opponents don't have enough to do everything they want either. Scarcity creates tension. You don't need to prove and publish every theory on the board, you just need to score more points than the second-rate scholars across the table.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.3
(+) Immersive theme: this game truly feeds your hunger for experiment and discovery

(+) Exciting boardgame experience: it has every player involved from the first minute to the last

(+) Beautiful artwork: the game looks and feels fantastic

(+) Familiar game mechanics drive the core of the game, but they are presented in such a way, intertwined with deduction aspects to make for a very original game experience

(+) Humor, always a big plus

(+) Very reasonable game time, little downtime, smooth game flow

(+) An additional plus because the game made me feel 'happy' for a number of non-specific reasons :) It is such a cute and charming game!

(-) Some luck dependence with early experiments. You want to make sure to get some insight into blue, green and red aspects as soon as possible and in the beginning of the game you will have to realize this with trial and error. Such luck-dependence usually puts me off, but in the case of Alchemists I can easily put this aside as those early 'trial and error' experiments are fun to carry out and fit the theme of the game perfectly. Furthermore, luck dependence creeps into the core of this game as it is all about taking (really thouroughly calculated!) chances. Furthermore, some luck dependence which can not be mitigated enters play with some artefact cards, drawing favor cards and obtaining the right ingredients (limited open card row).

(-) Quality of components is actually rather good: thick cardboard, beautiful artwork, very sound and beautiful player screens. However, I 'm not sure how many game sessions they might last: the way the player screens fold kind of freaks me out, I'm not sure how long the result-tokens will last taking into account you need to punch them in and out of your triangles each time and they only delivered a notebook for 25 4p-game sessions...
-> CGE has put a webform on their website with information on how to obtain 2nd print result markers which fit the deduction triangle better. More info over [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1254086/problem-counters]here[/url].

Tips:
(*) An early publish of a wrong theory is risky, certainly in the apprentice variant. If others have already experimented with that component and they have knowledgde of an aspect of which you are unsure, chances are big that your theory will get debunked in one or two rounds. Don't waste your action and money on an early publish unless you can use it to snatch away a scholarship.
(*) It's easy to go low on money in the game. It is difficult to obtain money. (selling requires the right ingredients, preferably a place early in turn order and 2 action cubes!). You need money in order to publish theories (and you will need more money of you publish later than necessary) and if you want to buy artefacts.
(*) So far we have only played the apprentice variant. I think the master variant will be much better, giving a better dynamic of the debunking action.

Approx playtime: +- 40 min / player

RATING: 7
05-12-2014. Rated after 1 play. Relatively heavy eurogame which combines worker placement with a deduction element. 

In Alchemists you are trying to correctly mix potentions and determine which ingredients can be combined to make a positive, negative or neutral potion. Then, you try to earn points by making potions and positing scientific theories on the polarity of the ingredients. This part of the games uses worker placement.

We played using a misprinted Essen copy, but otherwise the components were fine. One 'geekrage' eliciting aspect of the game is that an APP is used to determine which the configurations of ingredients are. To me, that's no issue and if you find someone willing they made a cardboard version who a dedicated non-player can be used to replace the app.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
http://www.asaboardgamer.com/review/alchemists/
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The app implementation with visual card recognition is very well done.  Unfortunately, it's not a good deduction game if that's what you're looking for.  The deduction part is very simple and easily solved if you invest your time in testing, but the game is really won on establishing hedged guesses early, endorsing others, and being most efficient with your action cubes in converting them to VPs (with gaining ingredients and testing being generally an inefficient path to VP).  Unfortunately, the hedging isn't that interesting either; since hedging wrong is a significant VP loss, you're only going to do it when you're confident it won't be wrong (you know 2 alchemicals and just unsure about the third).  This is very different than the feeling of risk in Mystery of the Abbey where you may risk declaring a prophecy when you are just playing the odds hoping that the murderer is fat or bearded for example.  Personally, I just wish the game had been much more difficult in the deduction part (more ingredients, more complex interactions between ingredients, mixing of 3 or more,...) and had more development in the types of testing (different students you can test on, carryover effects on students positive or negative from previous rounds, drinking a good potion yourself giving you a bonus, instead of selling a potion for gold there are a variety of different wizards that appear each round that will answer different types of questions about the ingredients Sleuth-style, just to name some random ideas) and really centered around testing and deduction.  I really wanted there to be more meaningful ways to gain information from the results of other players' testing/questioning.  And the different + potions have names (IIRC speed, power, and ....) but these have no relevance at all to the game!  I want the potions to be cool and meaningful.

Oh, and I like the Fresco-like selection of a wake-up time for turn order, but unlike Fresco where this interacts with the tension of trying to second guess where other players are going to place workers, this has a weird theme-disconnect where the player waking up latest places his worker cubes on the board first, but those workers will activate last, so the other players can easily just block that player by going before them, but the late riser's first worker gets to go before the early riser's second worker, and selling a potion turn order doesn't matter as much because you're all going to bid for the highest discount, and.... it just is a real mess thematically and using secret simultaneous cube placement like in Fresco I think would have been a great idea and much faster. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is great and innovative.Players are  magical alchemists trying to find the chemical makeup of certain ingredients. Players then race to publish their theories to receive grants and fame. The most famous alchemist wins.

Pros:Tight tension competition, great worker placement integration, brilliant integration of the app, beautiful artwork and fun app

Cons: long gameplay especially for indecisive players, requires app to play ,new game concepts can be difficult to teach.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The first time I played this game, my brain nearly melted. I had to ask how deduction worked over and over again. Nevertheless, I was completely enamoured with Alchemists.

App implementation is brilliant, it's neither a gimmick, nor is it distracting you from the essence of gameplay. Without the app, this game would not be possible (even though the rules give you an alternative in which [-]your worst enemy[/-] a non-playing friend can take on the role of the app.

The deduction feels like a sudoku puzzle. The first few games, the thrill of deducing as much about as many ingredients as possible matters more than scoring points. The action selection/worker placement is great, you'll always wish for just one more action. And failing at the deduction doesn't mean you don't stand a chance at winning the game. There's many ways to score points, even when you have limited knowledge about the ingredients. Each time you play, you notice you've done better than the previous time you played.

Oh, and the artwork is great too. I can't help but think about Unseen University from Terry Pratchett's Discworld novels, when I look at it.

Experienced players do have an edge over newbies, though. But the aforementioned fun of deducing the elements will matter more to new players than actually winning the game. I recommend new players start out in a 2-player game, as you'll have more actions and thus errors are less punishing.

But all things considered: I love this game!

RATING: 8.5
There may be other games which rival it in coolness of theme, but I don't think any game achieves the marriage of theme and mechanics so well as Alchemists. I love that when I say things like "I'm not sure whether I want to publish this theory based on only one experiment and the as-yet unproved research of my opponent", I am describing exactly what is going on both in theme and mechanically

The game runs so well, and the deduction in the game is an ingenious design. It's a fantastic feeling when you take two ingredients which you have never mixed before, yet you are able to predict the result based on the relationships to other ingredients you have worked with. The worker placement aspect of the game is fine, and is a good enough backdrop for the superb systems for which it serves as a backdrop.

RATING: 3
-Teach time is far too long. The CGE [u]employee[/u] who explained the game to us took 60 minutes. An expert teacher could probably do it in 30 minutes, but it's still far too high of a learning curve. I have no interest in spending over 20 minutes to learn any game, no matter how good it might be.

-The game itself is far too long for what it is. Lots of parts feel convoluted, like they felt like the deduction game wasn't enough, and started to just throw in other mechanics (worker placement, auction, bluffing...etc.) to add more "game". Every Euro mechanic but the kitchen sink made it into this game.

-Artifacts seem wildly unbalanced and untested, even some fans admit this.

-The most unforgiving game I've ever played. The smallest of errors means 3 hours are down the drain. I am okay with a small error destroying you in a deduction game, but don't make it take 3 hours if it's going to be that unforgiving.

-Even if you make no actual "mistakes", the game can come down to one or two 50/50 chances you take. The game is waaaaaaaay too long for this sort of coin flip to victory.

Overall, the length is the main problem here, and most of my problems with the game stem from that.Too long and unforgiving.

RATING: 8.25

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I believe this is the heaviest weighted game we have in our collection at the moment. I see many people trading their copies of Alchemists, many unopened or unpunched. I think this is a huge mistake! This game is incredible. It is equal parts deduction and worker placement. The rules are quite intimidating but the game offers 2 variants for players just starting out and players who are more confident with the gameplay. This game's use of smart phones/tablets/computers is awesome. All players can share on device or use their own personal devices by inputting a shared code that is randomly generated. This code is linked to the game's deduction element and ensures that all player's combination of ingredients generate the same potion results. This game is definitely for experienced gamers but it is one of the most beautifully striking games we own. This one definitely needs considerable table space though!

RATING: 9.5
I love this game. It's a Euro. It's worker placement. It's deduction.

But doesn't it fail the glance test mentioned in 7 Wonders? Kinda. Each player does have a public board to display resources and vague information they have gathered. What the game does have is what I'll call the Tank test - the player has to go into the "Tank" of their mind and sort out what to do. And this game demands that from the player because you can't really get much help with the information behind your screen.

Note: 7 Wonders does this too, but feels like more of Glance game than a Tank game. Maybe the best way to define the difference is in how a game allows players to respond or compete with each other. Do players need to keep glancing to calculate a response or to go into the tank to calculate one?

RATING: 6
[2015-07-04]
Timbuktu szebb kinézettel és komolyabb körítéssel. Egy dedukciós játékot kapunk, amibe belecsempészték a jó öreg munkáslerakós mechanizmust. 8 főzetről kell kiderítenünk a három pontos alapanyagát. Ezt úgy tudjuk megtenni, hogy kísérletezünk adott kettő összeöntésével, ami után egy app megmondja, hogy a kettő milyen kölcsönhatásban van egymással. Ebből következtethetünk a két főzet egyes összetevőire. A munkáslerakós rész csak körítés tulajdonképpen, a lényeg a kevergetés. Miután egyre több információnk van megpróbálunk javaslatot tenni a pontos összetételre. Ezt tehetjük blöffölve akár rosszul is, ezzel megkeverve az ellenfeleket, de mehetünk tuti biztosra is. Véleményem szerint a tuti biztos stratégia egyrészt unalmas, másrészt szerencsével verhető. A játékban van két fajta bónuszkártya. Az egyik kisebb segítséget jelent egy-egy körben, a másik fajta pénzbe kerül és érzésem szerint elrontja a játékot a túlzott beavatkozásával. Hihetetlen erősnek érzek egy-egy ilyen kártyát és négy játékos esetén csak három szerezhet belőle az első körben és akkor sem biztos, hogy a véletlenül felcsapott három mind erős. Azaz adott esetben csak a kezdő játékos kap jó kártyát. A másik gondom a játékkal az újrajátszhatóság. Elsőre persze érdekes az apphasználat és a kevergetés, de többedikre én unalmasra saccolom. A játék szép, a téma mondjuk átjön, de valahogy nem éreztem átütőnek az összképet és rontó dolgok is voltak, amikről írtam. Ha valaki hibázik benne, akkor azzal elszállhat az egész játéka, nem megengedő a hibák terén. Érzek benne runaway leader problémát is. Nem rossz, de én köszönöm nem kérem a gyűjteményembe.

RATING: 10
Two of my favorite things combined... Worker placement and deduction. I think the flavor of this game is awesome (aided by a GREAT rulebook). What really set it apart is the publishing theories aspect. You're encouraged to publish early because whoever has the most theories/endorsements gets a bonus point at the end of every round. But you're also encouraged to debunk theories to gain 2 points. So when publishing early theories, if you hedge your bet correctly, you can gain 4 points from one ingredient (at the cost of 2 gold).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
You can add this to the list of games I adore despite not necessarily being something I often gravitate towards.  I'm not the biggest fan of deduction games but despite that I was always kind of excited for this game because of the integration with an app that is actually far from just a gimmick but makes each game variable.  I love genuine replayability in my games and this one offers a lot of that in a really beautiful, elegant, and fun package.

As stated above, this is a deduction game.  The players are all alchemists and the game includes 8 ingredients.  Each of these 8 ingredients is made up of a set of 3 properties.  When combining two ingredients it makes 1 of 7 possible potions and this information allows you to deduce the properties of each of the 8 ingredients.  The actions in this game are all taken via pseudo-worker placement.

I really love the turn order choosing phase of this game.  Very much like Fresco you can choose where to go in turn order, going later gets you more stuff than going earlier.  However, they beefed up the advantage of going first, not only do your actions resolve first but you also choose your actions last so you get information on where all the other players are going as well.  There are equal number of action spaces for each player, which is why I call it "pseudo" worker placement, but many of the spaces have advantages to going first.

The game is bright, colorful, and pretty.  Everything feels really sturdy and functional and the game even has a lot of thematic flare.  Also, the app integration is certainly not just a gimmick, it allows the aspects of each of the ingredients to be determined randomly each time you play without the need for a game manager who doesn't get to play.

I find the game not only extremely cerebral but fun and this combined with the unlimited replayability, beaufiful appearance, and reasonable ease-of-access make it a nearly perfect game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Clever deduction game where players are alchemists working to deduce the interaction of various components. It's an expensive life, so it's possible to earn money by (for example) selling potions, but the ultimate goal is prestige, accrued most notably by publishing theories (or disproving others' flawed ones). 

If I was left lukewarm is because this is all managed through a worker placement mechanism -- turn order is important etc. -- and so it's about as impure a deduction experience as you can get. The fact that it's possible to publish incorrectly, either deliberately as bluff or by erroneous educated guess, makes for a fascinating intersection: the reputation earned does have benefits for your engine, so even an incorrect guess may provide short-term benefits.

Unfortunately the result is reasonably complex and long enough to outstay its welcome. If you game as a social experience then it's important to know that this is not a game that may be problematic for players lacking attention to detail; the length compounds the issue common to most deduction games that incorrect record-keeping can easily put a player out of contention. 

There are also some mildly frustrating random elements (e.g. available artifacts) which may or may not reward turn order.

The novelty which attracted the most attention on release is the use of an app to provide appropriately partial information about how components interact, whether a theorem is proved/disproved etc. It's extremely well done, with an excellent interface, image recognition to avoid data entry etc., and so the game is worth playing if just to demonstrate how apps really can seamlessly enhance a social board game experience. The requirement to have a human moderator has always been a problem for hidden-information games.

My main criticism is, as ever, that the mashup of worker placement and deduction is less than the sum of its parts. It's enjoyable enough a combination, but I'd prefer to play a purer deduction game, like Letters from Whitechapel, Tragedy Looper or Hanabi, or an efficiency game without the deduction.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The deduction challenge at the center of this game is really obfuscated by the "big/little" and "+/-" differentiators.  The visual challenge of distinguishing between the faintly printed, tiny plus and minus signs didn't improve the situation.  A reasonably clever and interesting game if you can get past that and get your smartphones synched.  On second playing:  Dropping the rating by a point.  There is too much stuff going on in this game, too many rules.  Granted, in order to make a game out of an exercise in deduction, they needed to throw some rules at it.  But they've overdone it.  For example, in the last round (and the last round only) there's an option to fulfill a recipe for a point, and to fill the recipe for the +/- of the same color for another point, and if you can fill them both, it's worth two points.  But you have to be the first one to do it to get the single point for fulfilling a recipe -- but you DON'T have to be the first one to do it to get two points for filling them both.  Nothing about this brace of rules improves the game; it just slows down the learning and the playing.

RATING: 8.5
I love this game so much! I really enjoy the simulation of academia (probably not that surprising since I am in academia). Everything is so thematic while still having solid euro mechanics. I love the logistics of collecting your resources for the experiment, testing your potions on a student or yourself (and maybe suffering the consequences!), and using deduction (and sometimes induction) to determine the composition of the ingredients to publish your theories. I also love having to reason about what kinds of ingredients your want to publish on so as to establish yourself in the field and get grant money. It's especially great that even if your theory is debunked, you still get to keep the money from the grant!

Like I said, I love this game. However, the deduction aspect gets a bit easy and repetitive somewhat quickly (especially if you have familiarity with formal logic). I will probably be picking up the expansion soon in an attempt to make the deduction a bit more challenging... or at least make the decisions of what to research more challenging since you'll be spread a bit more thin and you won't have the resources to do it all.

RATING: 7.5
Very interesting and unique game of deduction and calculated risk taking.  It's like Clue on steroids.  The theme is awesome, the integration with the smart phone app is brilliantly done and game-play is engrossing.  I've only played it once and I enjoyed it, even though I really didn't have much clue what I was doing.

Essentially you are trying to figure out which compounds make up which ingredients.  You accomplish this by mixing ingredients and seeing what they make, thus allowing you to slowly eliminate possible compounds for ingredients off your deduction grid.  It's basically a big logic problem where all the unknowns are randomly generated by the smart phone app and clues are slowly given out throughout the game, by the app.

But there is a lot more than just a logic problem going on here.  It's a clever worker placement game as well, filled with bluffing and trying to outmaneuver opponents.  And the point system highly encourages publishing theories before you are sure of something, which adds luck and the penalty for guessing wrong can be pretty steep.  But it's all about taking calculated risks and hedging your bets correctly.

I did very poorly in my first game and found out we had played part of it wrong so I left with a skewed impression of it.  But now that I have played a few games on boiteajeux.net (great online version of it) I feel more at ease with the game and look forward to trying it again with our gaming group.  But be warned, there are a lot of rules in this game (even though the rule book is well written and even comical) and you will find yourself going over a section of the rules several times before you wrap your brain around it.  I would recommend trying it online first.  That is the best way to learn it.

I'll be revisiting this review after a few more plays with my game group.

RATING: 9
Played it twice, very nice.

It's novel combination of several game mechanics, and it works out well. The basics are 1) worker placement and 2) deduction a la Cluedo/Clue. In addition, the game uses a smart phone app which plays the role as a gamemaster of sorts, the only one knowing the makeup of each ingredient and thus the resulting effect of mixing two of the eight availible ingredients. The app worked well for us.

First impressions: Correct deduction is the key to winning, and some amount of bluffing is therefore essential versus experienced players, If your opponents know that you are never bluffing, it will be too easy for them drawing conclusions from your actions. However, each bluff is risky since you can and will get debunked. Aquiring a reputation as a some who bluffs all the time, though in practice you actually don't, seems like good strategy to win the psychological game.

I won my two games, scoring above 30 and 40, and I believe that the only thing I did better than my opponents, who were all experienced gamers though new to the game just like myself, was drawing conclusions from their actions. I always published lots of theories that I wasn't sure of, but guessed based on my opponents actions, and ignored artifacts until the final round where some optimization (Agricola-style) is required to maximize your score.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An amazing new generation game that flawlessly combines euro and thematic elements while efficiently exploiting a smartphone application to minimize bookeeping.

This is a heavy strategy game, one that combines action selection with heavy deduction, minor bluffing and push your luck in a way that truly delivers. The theme is the game, and you really feel like an Alchemist, agonising for the result of every expiriment, trying to figure out what is what by pushing your students, your clients and even yourself over the limits all the time. The whole publication "mechanism", full with its grands and diprovals is simply awesome. The interaction in this game is great, as half the game is action selection with an extremelly important player order and the other half is trying to understand what the other players really know and use it to your advantage. Downtime is a non-issue for anyone who is interested in the game, as the deduction part is so engaging and logical that you can get lost in it even if your opponents take an eternity to play. (which is not the case anyway - the actions are simple enough for anyone with half a plan). The application solves like magic the issue of complexity, and since the bookeeping is also solved with excellent components the game really sucks you in it.

So anything to complain about? Well, this is not the game to take with you in the deserted island, as with no power supply you just can't play it. :) For all those app-haters, please think of the app as your automated "dungeon master": It builds the world for you and reavels it gradually, based on your actions - it really serves the boardgame. I can see people hate this game however - whoever finds the deduction part tiring and uninteresting will have a 2.5h ride of pure boredom.

This game amazed me, even if I didn't really expect it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

-Scales well 

Absolutely my favorite heavy deduction game

What I don't like:

RATING: 6
I knew going into this game that it was "the hotness" and that at least one person needed an app to play it. I was a little worried that it would be hokey because of that. As it turned out, I rather enjoyed that aspect of the game.

We're alchemical researchers who are gathering ingredients, testing concoctions on students (or in dire straits, on ourselves), publishing theories (where you want to be first author for more reputation), debunking other's theories, and selling potions to whoever will pay.

The game is a fairly standard worker placement game, but there's a turn order auction every round and you have to place all your workers out in one go, in reverse turn order. So going last means other people can swoop in after you and make your placements look stupid. And then there's the app/deduction aspect.

A large part of the game revolves around a deduction mechanic. And this is where the app comes in. When you drink a potion, you place the two ingredients on the table behind your screen and line the app up over them. It tells you what potion you've made. Poisoning, paralyzing or driving your student insane means they'll need to be bribed to quaff any further potions (so being the first to conscript the student for the round is a good idea). And drinking something vile yourself has dire consequences.

Once you know what potion you've made, there's a pyramidal chart in your player board that you plug the corresponding chit into. And you have a sheet with a table where you can mark your inferences. You'll want to deduce the properties of each ingredient which lets you know what potions it can make with other ingredients (or vice versa, knowing what potion two things make can tell you the properties of those ingredients). For some reason this part of the game didn't click with me until halfway through the game, but it's not particularly difficult or onerous. Figuring out what potions are shouldn't be too much of a problem.

At the end of the game there's an exhibition, where you can demonstate which potions you've figured out, but only the first person to display a potion gets any fame. And at the end of the game all the theories are evaluated and you get points for authoring correct theories.

Initially I liked this game a lot... I loved the theme, and the deduction aspect was an interesting twist to the game. It really added to the feeling of being an alchemist figuring out how to make various potions.  But the more I play it the more I realize that I just don't like the deduction part of the game.  It's like you're playing a worker placement game while solving a puzzle, and I just don't have that much interest in puzzles.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2
I really wanted to like this game but I just had a terrible experience for a variety of reasons-- some of those my fault, some others, and some the games.  I don't know if I'd call this bad game, but it's definitely not a good one.  Just a mediocre game in a good looking game's disguise with a slick app.

I played this at BGG.CON 2014 and my first problem was the two people teaching us-- they were just bad teachers.  Not the games fault at all, but it took them about an hour and a half to teach us the game when it shouldn't have taken more than 20-30 minutes.  It was almost comically bad how bad these two people were at teaching games.

Anyways, within the first turn I was already in trouble.  See, I had the misfortune of going to last and the two people that knew how to play had the luck of going first.  One guy immediately bought the artifact that essentially double his points (gleefully pointing out that the item won him the last game he played) and the second guy happily bought the artifact that let him throw away only one ingredient when mixing potions.

Lets just take a moment to marvel at how broken both of these items are that you can buy on the *first turn*.

On one hand, you have an item that essentially doubles your points (until the very end of the game).  That is just completely ridiculous.  The only way you can even compete with that is if you have something that miraculously gives you elements...like an artifact that lets you keep half your ingredients.  Yes, we had one person that doubled their points every time they scored and another that had to forage only half as often as everybody else.  Needless to say, me and the other person were hopelessly behind.  Even worse, we were hopelessly behind for no reason other than we had the misfortune of not being first.

And that brings about the next problem-- there's no rubber banding in this game.  Games like this need rubber banding.  A game like Power Grid lets the person in last place do everything first because otherwise the person ahead will just get further ahead and leave everyone in the dust.  That's what happened in my game!

So anyways, I was last which meant I got to experiment on the student last.  Which meant I had to waste gold on that.  Which meant I to transmute more elements.  Which meant I had to forage more often.

So what do you do when you run out of gold?  Well, you experiment on yourself!  Unfortunately this game has a large luck factor in those beginning rounds and luck was not on my side.  See, I had this fantastic ability to only find green negative potions when experimenting on myself...which meant I still was last even when I was first player.  So I was behind the whole game and it was miserable.

To make things worse, I messed up marking the results at one point and didn't realize until an hour later.  When I realized this, I of course checked my sheet and everything seemed right, which meant I put a peg in a wrong spot.  Yes, it was my fault but that one _easy_ mistake meant it was literally impossible for me to compete because everything I knew was wrong, I would have to retest *everything* to get valid information again, and it was just hopeless.  As complicated as this game is, it needs to handle player mistakes more gracefully.  When it's getting near 12:30AM and you just realized you've lost all chances of even coming in third, you really start questioning whether you want to ever play that game again.

So I sat there miserable for the next hour watching the two people who got the good magic items compete and I realized something-- there's literally zero player interaction in this game!

All three other players had their heads buried behind their screens the entire time looking at their sheets.  I constantly had to remind them to go (because I for sure didn't want that game to last any longer).  Again I bring up Power Grid, but at least in something like that you're competing over space or auctioning, in this, there's really nothing.  The person who places cubes first just accepts they're screwed and then goes back to fiddling with their sheets.

So I know I started off saying this game wasn't terrible, but the more I think about it the more I realize I hated this game.  It's just not a good game, it only looks like one.

RATING: 7.5
Wow this game has a lot off layers.  Not just the testing and deduction, but the race to publish, the battle for turn order, the mini game for selling potions, different paths to victory... This game can make your head spin. At the same time once you start solving the puzzle, you're grinning ear to ear at your own ingenuity.  

What a great and thematic spin on what is basically a worker placement game at it's core, with deduction worked around it and some neat action space mechanisms.

Bonus points in that I don't think I've ever had so much fun discussing a game after the fact.  The theme is worked into everything.

There is a fair amount of downtime, but I never for a second felt bored since there was always things to plan ahead for future turns and educated guesses to make on the deduction screen.

The downside of the game is really in how long it takes to teach, how many different elements there are to wrangle in your head, and therefore how difficult it is to pick up in your first play.  It takes 2 plays to really get on the same page with an experienced player.  the game can be very unforgiving where one deduction mistake or misplanned action can really ruin you.  It can be a bit too stressful for that reason so it's a game I'd have to be in the mood for.

RATING: 9
One of the most interesting and unique games I have ever played. Deduction problem combined with worker placement.

At 4 players becomes an incredibly tight worker placement game with lots of opportunity to block people. It's a bit more open with less players, but at 4 I would call this by far the most player interaction I have ever seen in a euro.

The race to publish results first provides excellent incentive to guess or bluff allowing players to create a rich layer of intrigue of thier own. Do you trust something others have published? Do you know that something published is wrong but your not quite confident you can prove it? Do you just blatently make stuff up in order to win prestige and grants at the risk of losing face later?

The theme is excecuted magnificently and the game is genuinely funny on many occasions.

Only flaw that holds me from playing more often is that the rules are a bit bloated and teaching the game to new players can be annoying. I would put this in the "gamers only" category due to complexity and difficulty. Would have been perfect if they could have streamlined a few things just a little.

Expansion added some great mechanisms for variable resources I consider these modules auto includes and a must buy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Sudoku for imbeciles deduction game driven by a mediocre worker placement game buried in randomness, more randomness, and even more randomness.

A game of pointless deduction, mediocre worker placement, and random randomness with randomness on top.

Looked interesting and it is worth a try for the innovative phone/tablet app integration which works well, but unfortunately the game sucks. Basically advanced "Mastermind" burried under a mountainous mess of mechanisms and randomness upon randomness drawn out over 2-3 hours.

Interest dwindled rapidly already during the seemingly neverending setup and rules explanation.

Components wise, the illustrations are great but the board is far too busy and looks like a mess. The player screens are completely moronic with vertical deduction boards where the markers do not fit in the holes and are bound to fall out; and a tiny ledge where you are supposed to balance your ingredients cards for tests and then photograph them with the phone/tablet app which is an utterly pointless gimmick that is vastly slower than simply tapping on the two relevant cards in the app. But apart from this useless gimmick, at least the app works well and it would probably be a nightmare to handle the test results manually.

A completely and easily, methodically solvable deduction puzzle only limited by eventual initial ignorance of the steps to solve it; and ultimately by the randomly available/drawn ingredients cards, and the random "+" or "-" outcome of lab tests where "-" may arbitarily cost you a penalty, and the outcomes of uncertain client test may randomly give you more or less useful test results and eventually rewards or penalties.

When you know something from your tests, then the random test results and the randomly available/drawn ingredients cards may or may not match the randomly drawn client tile needed to get the gold needed to place your deducted solutions needed to score points.

What it boils down to is lucky draw of ingredients and lucky test outcomes to get a headstart through the run of the mill worker placement part to place the first, cheaper deduction results and pick up bonus scoring tiles. That is not much of a game for 2-3 hours.

.....

Clearly, I need to practice my getting random results skills:

My first round's two tests both completely randomly gave "-" results which are arbitrarily punished with a penalty ("+"/"-" are not "good"/"bad" but could just as well be "x"/"y" or anything else instead - the arbitrarily penalised "-" are simply defined as being poison for thematic fun).

My first round's two tests were also completely randomly both "blue" which gives you less information and which reduces your chances of matching the subsequent rounds' client demands.

The second round's completely random client demand tile happened to want "blue+" (or something red or something green), so no way for me with only two "blue-" to make a certain bid for a full reward but would instead have to settle for a partial bid for only a small reward, or to simply make a random two-to-one guess and risk another penalty.

Anyway, even the two-to-one guess was not possible as none of my five randomly drawn/available ingredients cards matched my test combinations' previous four ingredients cards. So my only option for a good reward would be a completely random fifty-fifty guess and risk another penalty - or do some additional testing instead and wait until next round where things were bound to be better .....

Yeah, right .....

Four of my first five test results all completely randomly turned out to be "blue-" (and a "green+") for further penalties and reduced information from another double "blue-" test. Seven of my nine test results all completely randomly turned out to be "-" for yet more penalties throughout the game.

The third round's completely random client demand tile again happened to want "blue+" or "green-" (or something red), so again no way for me with only four "blue-" and one "green+" to make certain bid for a full reward but would instead still have to settle for a partial bid for only a small reward, or to simply make a random two-to-one guess and risk another penalty.

Again, none of the third round's four randomly drawn/available ingredients cards matched any of my previous test combination's ingredients so still only possible to make completely random fifty-fifty guesses for client demands.

And blah blah blah .....

Knowing the correct, full solutions for six of the eight recipes after four (three and a half) rounds of absolutely no value scrambling to finally get the ingredients to get some gold to publish my solutions, many of which by now costs extra because of other players' already published solutions.

.....

At least there is half an hour or so of fun to be had by figuring out the few simple methodical steps to solve the puzzle part, and fortunately you do not need buy or play this very poor game to do so - simply download one of the deduction sheets and the iOS app. You can squeeze out a bit more fun also figuring out probabilities for yet unsolved parts. But after you have figured out these simple steps (and optionally probabilities), the deduction part of the game will be purely mechanical and of absolutely no interest, leaving you with just a very random, mediocre worker placement game.

RATING: 9
I really love this game.  Having played several games, with scores all over the map and varying outcomes every time, I feel that it provides plenty of variability, even after players have become comfortable with the rules.
The margin for user error is very high, with the deduction grid for starters, and also the strategic planning to stretch your ingredients and gold through each turn.  But it's this constant challenge that makes the game so immersive and so exciting.  Often you find yourself realizing a plan you put in place several actions ago just crumbled at your feet, and just when you think you have it all down pat, an opponent disproves your theory (which you were quite confident in) and suddenly you're struggling to regain composure - and points!
Alchemists also has one of the most entertaining instruction manuals I've ever read - its both witty, and does a fantastic job explaining the thematic elements of its rules.
A truly fantastic and whimsical game.

RATING: 7
