RATING: 10
When I was in elementary school I was obsessed with the game clue. I would solo play that thing to death. The deduction intrigued me, and it's why so many years later I picked up this game without reading into much of anything. 

Then I opened the box, saw the weight of this game, and shoved it away. 6 months later my wife and I finally decided to put this on the table, and to our surprise, this was a really easy learn! It took us about an hour to have the rules completely figured out. 

The key was to understand and engage in the flavor behind each action. The rules to alchemists are so masterfully designed and fit the narrative so easily, that we were able to play the entire game after one rules read through and not refer back to the rules until scoring.  Read the flavor behind each action, come up with your own flavor where necessary, and you won't question any rules!

I don't like a lot of Euro style games, but this one tickled me in a way where it just felt like so much more than your standard euro. I love the way my wife and I interact when we play this game. She even found herself contesting her own results on her first playthrough! Which I found hilarious.

RATING: 4.5
I was really excited to play this game because it looks so fabulous and it seemed to be "logically interesting".
I won my very first (and only) game.
Despite these two very favourable conditions, i really didn't like the game.
The biggest problem is it's sooo terribly complicated and inelegant. The rules for matching elements are quite tricky and the explanation takes soooo long that i was already depressed before actually starting.
The math behind  the game is sound and the deduction process is working. At the end of the game we pretty much knew a lot about the ingredients despite "only" 6 turns.
Components are very nice to behold but the disks are difficult to handle for inept people like me.
I hate to be dependent on a smartphone to play a boardgame but i understand the need for a "master"...still, i prefer to play a master-less game if this is the condition to avoid a smartphone.
One of us got two neutral potions in the first 3 experiments... his game was ruined forever.
Luck plays such a big role that i really feel i don't deserve my victory completely.
The theme is of course nice and interesting but the gameplay is scarse under a number of aspects. 
All in all, a bad experience.

RATING: 8
Woooo boy, this was one heck of a game. I knew going into it that this would be meatier than most games I play and that analysis (paralysis) was part of the game. I had traded for it and it just sat on my shelf for weeks and months before I even decided to try it, but when I did I’m glad I spent the time learning it.

I’m not going to go too deep into this one because it’s pretty hefty, but you’re an alchemist and your job is to figure out what ingredients do what so you can publish theories, sell potions, and show off at the exhibition. You’ll do this by trying out your potion on customers, students, and yourself by combining two ingredients using an app on your phone or tablet.

In two games my wife and I have each won once, both times within one point of the other and both times we’ve really enjoyed it. It’s one of those games that captivated her after our initial play and she’s now looking for chances to get it to the table. I’d recommend trying this game first, but it’s definitely worth that try.

RATING: N/A
Budding alchemists in a race to discover the ultimate secret of the mystical arts.  A worker placement deduction game that relies heavily on a smart phone app to manage the mixing of potion components and privately revealing the clues in solving the puzzle. Fun theme, gorgeous artwork, and well thought out. It requires use of huge player screens to keep track of your deductions which I'm not a big fan of.

The use of technology to manage randomness and privately sharing information is clearly more efficient and vastly superior to the old fashion boards and bits, bags and dice.  But, one reason I love board gaming is to get AWAY from computers and cellphones.  People are already way too obsessed with using their phones to do everything.  I hope we are not seeing the writing on the wall--the future of board gaming will be on smart phones. At the very least, we are well on our way to a hybrid group. I'm sure the gaming world is big enough for all of us. Just please don't let the temptation of slick tricks of technology replace the joy of fiddling and social face-to-face interaction. Resist becoming a society of electronic interfacers.

RATING: 7.445
The heart of a Alchemists is a deduction puzzle similar to Cluedo but more interesting and with more of a learning curve.  Instead of Cluedo's roll-and-move mechanic (worst thing about Cluedo) you have have a worker placement mechanic instead... and it is not simple.  You will be buying and selling ingredients, making potions to test theories or sell to adventurers, buying items that give you special bonuses, publishing and refuting theories in order to gain prestige and academic grants, all leading to a convention where you can try to prove yourself the best at alchemy.  I really like this game, but the steep learning curve can make it tricky to introduce to new players.

RATING: N/A
4 after 1 play.

The game comes with huge amounts of downtime and very low player interaction. There seems to be a lot of parts that can be cut out from the game.

Firstly, selecting player position every round before actually placing your workers. That can be removed and just have players place workers in sequential order, but one worker at a time.

Secondly, collecting ingredients to sell by converting to gold or to adventurers for a small amount of gold, which one needs to publish. Why not just remove the need for gold to publish and remove the roundabout?

Thirdly, one may even simply remove gold from the game and make the deduction part of the game as its centerpiece.

Last but not least, it is rather sad that a board game requires a mobile phone app to play. The checking required during testing of ingredients and selling to adventurers induces more downtime and further inhibits interaction.

The game's components are high quality, though over-produced. The top piece above each player's screen is not required and blocks out part of the view of the main board.

RATING: 9
I love that solving the ingredients puzzle is not the whole of Alchemists, but rather part of the game. Usually deduction games fall apart because if one person accidentally gives a wrong answer, the game is ruined for all. Mess up an answer here, and you're (generally) hurting only yourself. I know Alchemists is on the heavier end of the spectrum, but I think it plays lighter than that. The decisions are straight forward, and there's a fun logic puzzle in the middle of it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2
One of the most rare elements in an Euro game is a theme that actually works. Well, with this one it really works. When playing, you are the guy on the cover of the box. You are mixing all those ingredients trying to find all of the solutions. Having said that, there is also the big minor point with this game. And that is the app. Everybody will say that you won’t have to use the app to play the game. Well, they are absolutely right. You won’t need an app. But when you don’t use the app, you will need somebody who is willing to stare at the gameboard for two hours. Watching every move that is made, doing nothing until somebody asks “What do I get when I mix Mandrake root and scorpion tail?” He can say two words then, and then, just wait again, until somebody will ask him a result of a mixture again. And all the time he’s just sitting there. Staring and thinking: “I want to play along!” To be short. Using the app will make the game easier and more fun for everybody. And that’s where another concern comes in. How long will the app be usable on a phone. I mean, 10 years ago nobody heard of an android OS. What kind of OS will a phone use in 10 years. Will there be an app for that OS by then? I really am afraid that there won’t be an app for it then. On the other hand, it shouldn’t be too hard to make a table with all the possibilities in a spreadsheet yourself. Just fill in the ingredients on page 1, and click on a button and that will give you the result. Is using the app necessary? Actually, yes it is. Enough about the app. It isn’t a novelty, but the implementation in the game is just great. It works perfectly (if you have enough light in the room you’re playing).

A 20 pages rule-book almost automatically means that this isn’t an easy game. Well, it isn’t easy, but it isn’t hard either. There is just a lot of keeping track of. The rules are written very clearly, and after reading them once you should be able to explain the game to somebody else. Please take in count that you will need about 30 minutes to do that.

Playing the game is quite an experience. It might take a couple of rounds before you understand the game, but then it flows naturally. The deduction part of the game is the main mechanic, and to us, it was completely new in a board game. After playing the game, I was overwhelmed by it. In a good way. Wow, this is just refreshing. This is something different. Let’s play it again!

RATING: 9
This is a great deduction game, possibly the best out there. It's a race to try to solve each of the eight ingredients and then publish those results, which leads to some interesting choices: should you try to publish early on a 50/50 shot of getting it right or wait one more turn to make sure you're correct, but also risk having someone publish that info before you can? The extra options like buying artifacts and selling potions make for lots of strategy since you can only do so many things per turn.

RATING: 8
A bit of worker placement, a touch of deduction, and an app for your smart device that essentially just randomises the puzzle. Really lovely components make this a feast for the eyes, and the game itself is at least intriguing, and at times very good.

I have a few concerns as to its longevity however, as I can't see how you'd approach the game too different time after time, but right now, I think this is a really solid one, that meshes board game and technology really well.
